Maturidi's Argument on Major Sinners and the Correct Irja'
Maturidi argues that major sinners have sufficient good deeds to avoid eternal punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that major sinners are not eternally punished because their good deeds outweigh their sins, and that their ultimate fate is to be left to God's will—the correct form of irja'.

Core Idea
A single good deed outweighs all minor sins, so major sinners are never eternally damned.

Maturidi addresses a critical debate: what happens to believers who commit major sins? He grounds his answer in the Quran and reason, arguing that such sinners possess good deeds that overpower their sins. Their ultimate fate is neither automatic forgiveness nor eternal punishment, but is entrusted to God's mercy and justice. This passage defines the correct understanding of irja'—postponing judgment to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the opening analogy to the Quranic verse about entrusting unknown matters to God.
This analogy sets the tone for irja'.
2
Identify the key premise
Find the sentence where Maturidi claims that one good deed of a major sinner would obliterate all sins less than shirk. Understand that this premise is the foundation of the argument.
This is a strong statement about the power of good deeds.
3
Explore the three possibilities
The passage presents three options for how God may deal with a major sinner: forgiveness, expiation through good deeds, or just punishment. List each one and note the supporting Quranic verses.
These are not mutually exclusive; they reflect God's choice.
4
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that this kind of irja' is true and necessary. Reflect on why he contrasts it with other views (e.g., Kharijite eternal punishment or Murji'ite suspension of faith judgment).
This irja' is about postponing punishment, not about suspending belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صادقين ﴾ [البقرة، ۳۱/۲] الآية ، إنه لما سئلوا عن أمر لم يكن لهم به علم، فوضوا الأمر في ذلك إلى الله . وكذلك الحق في أصحاب الكبائر، إذ معهم خيرات، الواحدة منها لو قوبلت جميع ما دون الشرك من الشرور لمحتها وأبطلتها. فلا يحتمل أن يُحرم صاحبها ويخلد في النار ؛ لكن يُرجَى أمره إلى الله، فإن شاء عفا عنه، إذ هو لم يحرمه عند فعله معرفته ومعاداة أعدائه له وتعظيم أوليائه . فعند شدة حاجته إلى عفوه وإحسانه يرجو أن لا يحرمه ـ والله الموفق ـ إذا قال: هو العفو الغفور ، وهو الرحيم الودود . وإن شاء قابل بسيئته ما أكرمه به من الحسنات فجعلهن كفارات لها، كما قال تعالى: إن الحسنات يذهبن السيئات ﴾ [هود، ١١٤/١١]، وقال في غير موضع نكفر عنكم سيئاتكم ﴾ [النساء، ٣١/٤]. وقد ذكر الأنواع التي وعد بها التكفير . ولا قوة إلا بالله . وذلك كقوله : أولئك الذين نتقبل عنهم أحسن ما عملوا ونتجاوز عن سيئاتهم الآية ، وقوله : والذين آمنوا وعملوا الصالحات لنكفّرن عنهم سيئاتهم ﴾ [العنكبوت ، ۷/۲۹]، ونحو ذلك . والله أعلم . وإن شاء جزاه قدر عمله، وما كان منه الحسنات فقدرها أيضاً بقوله: فمن يعمل مثقال ذرة خيرا يره الآية، وغير من ذلك من الآيات التي فيها ذكر جزاء الخير والشر. وذلك وصف العدل في المؤاخذة وإن كان هو فيما أعطى الثواب مفضلاً . وبالله التوفيق. وهذا النوع من الإرجاء حق لزم القول به.
The verse: 'If you are truthful' [Al-Baqarah 2:31], indeed when they were asked about something they had no knowledge of, they entrusted the matter to God. And likewise the truth regarding the people of major sins, for they have good deeds, one of which, if compared to all sins less than shirk, would obliterate and nullify them. So it is not probable that its owner be deprived and eternally in the Fire; rather his matter is postponed to God: if He wills, He pardons him, since He did not deprive him at the time of his action of His knowledge and enmity toward His enemies and magnification of His friends. So at his intense need for His pardon and beneficence, he hopes that He does not deprive him - and God is the Grantor of success - when he says: He is the Pardoner, the Forgiving, and He is the Merciful, the Loving. And if He wills, He counterbalances his evil deed with the good deeds with which He honored him, and makes them expiations for it, as the Exalted said: 'Indeed good deeds remove evil deeds' [Hud 11:114], and He said in another place: 'We will expiate for you your evil deeds' [An-Nisa 4:31]. And He mentioned the types which He promised expiation. And there is no power except with God. And that is like His saying: 'Those are the ones from whom We accept the best of what they did and overlook their evil deeds' [Al-Ahqaf 46:16] - the verse - and His saying: 'And those who believe and do righteous deeds, We will surely expiate for them their evil deeds' [Al-Ankabut 29:7], and similar to that. And God knows best. And if He wills, He recompenses him according to his deed, and what was of good deeds from him also He gives it its due measure by His saying: 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' [Az-Zalzalah 99:7] - the verse - and other verses that mention the recompense of good and evil. And that is the description of justice in accountability, although He is, in what He gives of reward, bountiful. And with God is success. And this type of irja' is true and necessary to affirm.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the fate of major sinners (ahl al-kaba'ir). Maturidi begins by drawing an analogy from the Quranic verse about those who said 'We have no knowledge' and entrusted the matter to God. Similarly, for major sinners, they have good deeds that are so weighty that a single one of their good deeds would obliterate all minor sins (sins less than shirk). Therefore, it is not possible that such a person be deprived of Paradise and be eternally punished in Hellfire. Instead, their matter is postponed (irja') to God's will: if He wills, He forgives them. Maturidi emphasizes that God did not deprive them at the time of their sin of knowing Him, opposing His enemies, and venerating His friends—these are good deeds that remain with them. So they hope for His pardon. Then Maturidi presents two possibilities: either God forgives (as His names al-'Afuww, al-Ghafur, al-Rahim, al-Wadud indicate) or He balances the evil with good deeds by making them expiations (kafarat), citing multiple Quranic verses (Hud 11:114, Nisa 4:31, Ahqaf 46:16, Ankabut 29:7, Zalalzalah 99:7). The third possibility is that He punishes in exact measure, which is justice, but He is bountiful in reward. Maturidi concludes that this form of irja' (postponing judgment about the ultimate fate of a sinful believer to God) is true and obligatory to affirm.

Key Terms

الإرجاء
التكفير
العفو
الحسنات
السيئات
العدل
الفضل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Irja' means suspending judgment about whether a major sinner is a believer or not, effectively denying that sins harm faith.
Correction: Maturidi's irja' specifically means postponing the punishment of a major sinner to God's will, not suspending belief in their faith. He affirms that such a person is still a believer despite their sins.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the fate of a person who commits major sins but also has good deeds?
A) He is eternally punished.
B) He is forgiven or punished according to God's will.
C) He is automatically forgiven.
D) He is declared an unbeliever.

Key Takeaway
Major sinners are not eternally punished because their good deeds outweigh their sins; their fate is left to God's will — this is the true meaning of irja'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have committed major sins. Do you truly believe that your good deeds, no matter how small, can outweigh them? If so, why do you still despair of God's mercy? What single good deed are you neglecting that could obliterate your sins?
?
When you think of a fellow Muslim who has fallen into major sin, do you secretly consider them doomed? How does this lesson challenge your attitude towards them? Will you change how you view them starting today?
?
If God were to bring you to account with perfect justice, would you be saved? How does the possibility of God's pardon or expiation make you feel? Are you living as if you expect mercy or as if you expect punishment? What will you do differently this week?

Maturidi's Interpretation of the Hadith on Qadariyya and Murji'a
Maturidi interprets the hadith about Qadariyya and Murji'a, explaining it refers to Jabriyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's interpretation of the hadith that threatens two groups with loss of intercession, how he identifies the Murji'a as the Jabriyya in this context, and the correct middle position on divine will and human action.

Core Idea
The hadith critiquing Qadariyya and Murji'a actually targets their extremes: denying God's will or attributing evil to Him.

This lesson examines a famous hadith about two groups that will not receive the Prophet's intercession. Maturidi provides a nuanced interpretation, showing that the Murji'a mentioned here are not the mainstream Murji'a but the Jabriyya who deny all human agency. Understanding this clarifies the correct Islamic position on divine will and human action.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and Maturidi's introduction
Begin by reading the hadith and Maturidi's statement that it can be interpreted in two ways. Notice that he focuses on one interpretation where 'Murji'a' means 'Jabriyya'.
The hadith is cited as a starting point for the discussion.
2
Understand the two erroneous views
Maturidi describes two opposing extremes: the Qadariyya attribute all power to creation, denying God's will, while the Jabriyya attribute everything to God, denying any real human agency.
These are the two groups condemned in the hadith according to this interpretation.
3
See the connection to Murji'a
Maturidi identifies the Murji'a in the hadith as the Jabriyya. This is because the Murji'a are often associated with irja' (postponement), but here they are paired with Qadariyya as opposites, pointing to fatalism.
This interpretation avoids conflating all Murji'a with error.
4
Recognize the middle way
The passage concludes by stating that the truth is the middle: actions come from the servants but are not independent of God's will. This balances divine sovereignty and human responsibility.
The passage is cut off, but the intended conclusion is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن ثبت الخبر المرفوع أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «صنفان من أمتي لا تنالهم شفاعتي: القدرية والمرجئة» ، وما ذكر أن المرجئة لعنت على لسان سبعين فهو يخرج - والله أعلم - على وجهين. أحدهما أن يراد به الجبرية، بما جمع إلى القدرية وهما قولان متقابلان جمعهما الخبر في الذم . وهو أن القدرية تحقق قدر أفعال الخلق للخلق، لا تجعل الله فيها مشيئة ولا تدبيرا، والجبرية أرجتها إلى الله تعالى لم تجعل للخلق فيها حقيقة ألبتة فحملت الجبرية كل قبيح وذميم على [الله] جل الله تعالى من أن يكون ذلك وصف فعله، وحملت القدرية الأمر على الخلق، على ما هم بها من الجهل. والحق هو الوسط من القول: أن تكون من العباد أفعال
Then if the raised hadith is established that the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, said: 'Two groups of my community will not receive my intercession: the Qadariyya and the Murji'a,' and what was mentioned that the Murji'a were cursed on the tongue of seventy, then it is interpreted - and God knows best - in two ways. One of them is that by it is meant the Jabriyya, because they are joined with the Qadariyya, and they are two opposing views that the hadith joined in blame. That is, the Qadariyya affirm the power of actions for the creation, not making God have any will or plan in them, while the Jabriyya attributed them entirely to God, not making for the creation any reality in them at all. So the Jabriyya attributed every ugly and blameworthy thing to God - exalted is God above that being the description of His action - while the Qadariyya attributed the matter to the creation, according to their ignorance. And the truth is the middle way: that actions come from the servants...
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage provides an interpretation of a hadith that threatens two groups with deprivation of intercession: the Qadariyya (those who affirm free will excessively) and the Murji'a (here understood as the Jabriyya or fatalists). Maturidi explains that the 'Murji'a' in this hadith likely refers to the Jabriyya, because they are opposites: Qadariyya attribute actions entirely to human power, while Jabriyya attribute all actions to God. The hadith condemns both extremes. The Qadariyya deny God's will and plan, while the Jabriyya attribute evil to God, which is impermissible. The correct position is the middle: actions are from the servants (i.e., they have real agency) but are not independent of God's will. The text is cut off, but the intended conclusion is clear.

المرجئة
الجبرية

المشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the hadith condemns all Murji'a without exception.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that the 'Murji'a' in this hadith refers specifically to the Jabriyya, not the mainstream Murji'a who postpone judgment.

Checkpoint

C) It means both groups are eternally punished.
D) It only applies to the Qadariyya.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi interprets the hadith as condemning both extreme Qadariyya (human autonomy) and Jabriyya (divine compulsion), and the correct view is the middle path where actions come from servants but are under God's will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Which false comfort do you rely on, and how will you move to the middle path that holds both God's sovereignty and your accountability today?
?

The First Interpretation: Qadariyya and Jabriyya as Opposites
Maturidi interprets the hadith, equating Murji'ah with Jabriyya and defining the middle truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's first interpretation of the hadith condemning Qadariyya and Murji'ah, specifically how he equates 'Murji'ah' with 'Jabriyya' and identifies the middle path between Qadariyya and Jabriyya.

Core Idea
The middle path: actions are from us, creation is from Allah.

This passage begins with a hadith threatening two groups with the Prophet's intercession. Maturidi offers a striking interpretation: when the hadith says 'Murji'ah', it may actually mean 'Jabriyya'—the fatalists. By pairing them with Qadariyya, the hadith highlights two opposing extremes, both misguided. The lesson then explores these extremes and reveals the balanced truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow: hadith, two interpretations, first interpretation elaborated, then the middle position.
The Arabic uses a clear argumentative structure.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Maturidi says the hadith has two interpretations. Focus on the first: that 'Murji'ah' here means 'Jabriyya'. Understand why he proposes this: because the hadith pairs them with Qadariyya as opposites.
This is the key exegetical move.
3
Understand the two extremes
Maturidi defines Qadariyya (full human capacity, no divine will) and Jabriyya (all from Allah, no human reality). Note how each extreme leads to problems.
Jabriyya attributes evil to Allah, which is unacceptable.
4
Grasp the middle truth
The middle position: actions are from the servants in terms of occurrence, but from Allah in terms of creation. This avoids both extremes.
This is the orthodox Ash'ari/Maturidi position on human action.
5
Reflect on the lesson's importance
Consider why this balanced view is crucial for Islamic theology: it preserves human responsibility while affirming divine sovereignty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن ثبت الخبر المرفوع أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «صنفان من أمتي لا تنالهم شفاعتي: القدرية والمرجئة» ، وما ذكر أن المرجئة لعنت على لسان سبعين فهو يخرج - والله أعلم - على وجهين. أحدهما أن يراد به الجبرية، بما جمع إلى القدرية وهما قولان متقابلان جمعهما الخبر في الذم. وهو أن القدرية تحقق قدر أفعال الخلق للخلق، لا تجعل الله فيها مشيئة ولا تدبيرا، والجبرية أرجتها إلى الله تعالى لم تجعل للخلق فيها حقيقة ألبتة فحملت الجبرية كل قبيح وذميم على الله جل الله تعالى من أن يكون ذلك وصف فعله، وحملت القدرية الأمر على الخلق، على ما هم بها من الجهل. والحق هو الوسط من القول: أن تكون من العباد أفعال على ما هي منهم، ومن الله خلقها على الحد الذي كانت عليه. وبالله التوفيق.
Then if the marfu' hadith is established that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'Two groups of my community will not receive my intercession: the Qadariyya and the Murji'ah,' and what was mentioned that the Murji'ah were cursed on the tongue of seventy [prophets] — then it has, and Allah knows best, two interpretations. One of them: that by it is meant the Jabriyya, because it is grouped together with the Qadariyya, and they are two opposing statements which the hadith combined in blame. That is: the Qadariyya affirm the capacity for the actions of the creatures to the creatures, not making Allah have any will or planning in them; and the Jabriyya attributed them entirely to Allah, not making for the creatures any reality in them at all. So the Jabriyya attributed every ugly and blameworthy thing to Allah — exalted is Allah from that being the description of His act — and the Qadariyya attributed the matter to the creatures, according to what they have of ignorance. And the truth is the middle position: that from the servants there are actions as they are from them, and from Allah is their creation in the manner they were. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage presents the first interpretation of a hadith that condemns both the Qadariyya and the Murji'ah. Maturidi notes that the hadith may actually refer to the Jabriyya (fatalists) when it says 'Murji'ah', because the two groups — Qadariyya (who affirm human power) and Jabriyya (who deny it) — are opposites, but the hadith pairs them in blame. He then explains each extreme: the Qadariyya assert that humans have full capacity for their actions, denying Allah's will and planning; the Jabriyya attribute all actions entirely to Allah, leaving no real role for humans, and thus attribute evil to Allah. The truth, Maturidi argues, is the middle position: actions come from the servants in terms of their occurrence, but from Allah in terms of their creation — a balanced view avoiding both extremes.

الجبرية
الوسط
الشفاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith condemns two separate groups: Qadariyya and Murji'ah, with no connection between them.
Correction: Maturidi shows that 'Murji'ah' in this hadith may refer to the Jabriyya, making the two groups opposing extremes (Qadariyya vs. Jabriyya) that are both blamed.

Checkpoint

D) Actions are neither from humans nor from Allah

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that the correct belief about human actions is a middle path: they are our actions in terms of occurrence, but Allah creates them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Creation of Faith: Proof and Refutation
Maturidi proves faith is created from its impossibility of being eternal and refutes deniers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical and scriptural proof that faith is created, and why denying this leads to a severe theological error.

Core Idea
Faith is not eternal; it is an act of the servant and is created by Allah.

Maturidi presents a rigorous argument that faith must be temporally originated, not eternal. Using both reason and revelation, he demonstrates that faith is an act of the servant, and therefore created. He then refutes those who deny this, showing that such denial leads to a position worse than dualism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Dichotomy
The passage begins by stating that faith must be either eternal (azali) or temporal (hadith). Recognize this starting point.
This sets up the logical framework.
2
Refuting the Eternal Option
Understand why faith cannot be eternal: it would mean faith exists before the person, which is impossible.
Maturidi says reason and scripture reject this.
3
Evidence that Faith is an Act
Look at the four pieces of evidence: command, prohibition, reward, punishment, and Quranic designation as 'amal (work).
These only apply to actions.
4
Conclusion: Faith is Created
Maturidi concludes that faith is the servant's act and is created, even more deserving than other acts.
Because it is the highest act.
5
Refutation of Deniers
Examine the comparison to Magians and Zindiqs, and why denying creation of faith implies God creates only evil.
This shows the severity of the error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لا يخلو - إذ علم - من أن يكون إيمان كل أحد يوصف في الأزل، أو بالكون بعد أن لم يكن. فإن لزم الوصف له بالكون في الأزل لزم الوصف بما في العقل دفعه وفي السمع إحالته، لإحالة كون إيمان أحد فعلاً له قبل كونه. والدليل على أنه فعل العبد الأمر به والنهي عن تركه، ومجيء الوعد لمن أتى به والوعيد على من أعرض عنه، ومحال كون ذلك كله على غير فعل؛ ثم الإخبار في القرآن عن الذي جاء به وتسميه ذلك عملاً وتسمية صاحبه به . والمعقول في ذلك أن يكون هو الذي يشهد بوحدانية الله ويؤمن برسله ويعتقد ذلك، وذلك أنه فعله . على أنه لو لم يكن فعله فيكون كسائر ما له مما لا صنع له فيه خلقاً عند الجميع؛ وإن كان فعله فهو عند القائلين بهذا أن كل فعل العبد مخلوق، وقد بيّنا ذلك فيما تقدم. فعلى ذلك الإيمان، بل هو أحق أن يوصف بالخلق من سائر أفعال العبد، إذ هو أعلى أفعاله وأجلها. ومن البعيد وصف الرب بخالق الأشياء الدنيئة والخبيثة وتنزيهه عن خلق الأشياء الرفيعة الحسنة، فيكون واصفه بهذا شراً من المجوس والزنادقة حيث أضافوا إلى الله خلق الخيرات ونفوا عنه خلق الشر، وهؤلاء نفوا خلق أرفع الخيرات وهو الإيمان. مع ما كان فيهم من يرى جميع الخيرات إيمانًا ، ثم لا يرى الله يخلق الإيمان، فيكون على قوله هو خالق كل شر، وليس بخالق خير ألبتة، جل الله عن هذا الوصف. ثم لا يخلو تعرّف الخلائق من أن يكون طريقها السمع من غير أن كان للعقل من ذلك نصيب، فيجب بمطلق القول خلق الإيمان بقوله : خالق كل شيء ، وهو شيء غير
Then it is unavoidable — since it is known — that the faith of everyone is either described as eternal, or as coming into existence after not having existed. If the description of it as existing in pre-eternity is necessary, then it would necessitate describing what reason repels and scripture deems impossible, because it is impossible that the faith of someone be an act of his before his existence. The evidence that it is the act of the servant is the command to do it and the prohibition against abandoning it, the coming of the promise for whoever does it and the threat against whoever turns away from it, and it is impossible that all of this be upon other than an act. Then the report in the Quran concerning what is brought by it and its designation as 'work' and the naming of its possessor by it. The rational in that is that it is what testifies to the oneness of God and believes in His messengers and holds that belief, and that is his act. And if it were not his act, then it would be like other things belonging to him in which he has no part, created according to everyone. But if it is his act, then according to those who say this, every act of the servant is created, and we have already explained that previously. Thus faith is like that; rather, it is more deserving to be described as created than other acts of the servant, for it is the highest and most noble of his acts. And it is far-fetched to describe the Lord as the Creator of base and vile things but to deem Him transcendent of creating noble and good things. So one who describes Him in this way is worse than the Magians and the Zindiqs, for they attributed to God the creation of good things and denied Him the creation of evil, while these [people] denied the creation of the most exalted good, which is faith. Moreover, among them is one who considers all good things to be faith, yet does not consider God to create faith. So according to his statement, He is the Creator of all evil, and not a Creator of any good at all. Exalted is God above such a description. Then the way the creatures are known cannot be other than through scripture, without reason having any share in that. So by the absolute statement, the creation of faith is necessitated by His saying: 'Creator of everything,' and it is a thing not...
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage is a proof that faith is created, followed by a strong refutation of those who deny its creation. Maturidi begins with a logical dichotomy: since faith is known, it must be either eternal (azali) or temporally originated (hadith). It cannot be eternal because that would mean faith exists before the person himself, which is rationally and scripturally impossible. He then presents evidence that faith is an act of the servant: it is commanded, prohibited, promised reward, and threatened with punishment—all of which only apply to actions. The Quran calls faith 'amal (work) and its possessor is named by it. Rationally, faith is the servant's act of testifying to God's oneness and believing in His messengers. If it were not his act, it would be like other involuntary things that are created by God without the servant's involvement. But if it is his act, then according to the position that all acts of the servant are created (as Maturidi argued previously), faith is created. In fact, faith is more deserving to be called created because it is the highest and most noble act. Maturidi then criticizes those who deny the creation of faith: they attribute to God the creation of base and vile things but deny Him the creation of the most exalted good, faith. This is worse than the Magians and Zindiqs (dualists) who at least attributed good to God and evil to another principle. These deniers end up saying God creates only evil and no good, which is a grave description. Finally, Maturidi invokes the general principle that the way creatures are known is through scripture, and the absolute statement (from Quran 6:102, 40:62) that God is the Creator of everything necessitates that faith, being a thing, is created.

Key Terms
أزل
كون
فعل العبد
خالق كل شيء
مجوس
زنادقة
إيمان
عمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying that faith is created is a minor theological error.
Correction: Maturidi shows it is worse than the position of Magians and Zindiqs, as it attributes only evil to God.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT presented as evidence that faith is an act of the servant?
A) Command and prohibition
B) Reward and punishment
C) Quranic designation as 'amal
D) The consensus that faith is eternal

Key Takeaway
Faith is a created act of the servant, and denying its creation leads to a grave misrepresentation of Allah as Creator of only evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your faith is an act that you perform, not just a state you find yourself in, then what active steps are you taking today to increase it? Are you treating your iman as something you must work on constantly, or have you become complacent?
?
Maturidi warns that denying Allah's creation of faith leads to attributing only evil to Him. Have you ever, even indirectly, thought that some good in your life came from other than Allah? What does this lesson demand you change in how you attribute every good, including your faith, to Him?
?
If faith is the highest and noblest act, yet it is created by Allah and done by you, how does this affect your sense of responsibility? Are you using your faith to obey His commands, or are you neglecting the very act that defines your relationship with Him? What one thing will you do today to make your faith more active?

Questioning the Denier and Hadith Evidence for Created Faith
Maturidi refutes deniers with a question and cites hadiths supporting created faith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the denial of created faith using logical questioning and prophetic traditions.

Core Idea
Faith is created because it has opposites, parallels, and is described as a created entity in hadith.

Imam Maturidi now turns to those who deny that faith is created. He begins by forcing them to clarify what faith really is—acknowledgment, confession, deeds, or something else. Once they admit it is one of these, he shows that all such things are created. He then supports his argument with two hadiths and a logical observation about opposites and similarities, concluding that faith is undeniably part of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument flow come through before you interpret.
Notice the questioning style at the beginning.
2
Identify the main components of the argument
The passage contains: (1) a question to the denier, (2) two hadiths, (3) a logical argument about opposites and forms, and (4) a list of descriptions of faith. Identify each part.
3
Understand the logical reasoning
The author argues that everything with an opposite and a similar is created. Since faith has an opposite (disbelief) and a similar (other created things), it must be created. Also, faith is called mercy, and mercy is created by Allah.
This is a kalam argument from attributes.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by listing that faith is a path, religion, doctrine, and belief—all of which are created. Ask yourself: why does this list strengthen the conclusion?
The list shows that faith is a human action or quality, hence created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنّا نسأل من أنكر ذلك سؤالاً مقررًا عن حقيقة ذلك: من تصديق أو إقرار أو جميع الأعمال أو إقرار ومعرفة أو نحو ذلك؟ فيلزم الاعتراف بشيء من ذلك بما يقابل به كل نوع ذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقد روى في ذلك خبر عن رسول الله عليه السلام أنه قال: «إن الله خلق الإيمان فحقه بالسماحة والحياء». وروى أن الله خلق مائة رحمة، ومعلوم تسمية الإيمان رحمة؛ فيجب أن يكون فيما خلق ثمة له ضد يدفعه وشكل يعضده أو يوافقه، وكل ذي ضد وشبيه خلق. ثم هو طريق يُسلك فيه، ودين يدان به ومذهب يختار، ونحلة تُعتقد، وكل ذلك مخلوق. ثم الله تعالى
Moreover, we ask the denier of that a firm question about its reality: is it acknowledgment, or confession, or all deeds, or confession and knowledge, or the like? So he is compelled to admit something of that by which every type of that is confronted. And there is no power except through Allah. And it has been reported in that a tradition from the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, that he said: 'Indeed Allah created faith and surrounded it with generosity and modesty.' And it is reported that Allah created a hundred mercies, and it is known that faith is called mercy; so it must be that among what He created there is for it an opposite that repels it and a form that supports or agrees with it, and everything with an opposite and a similar is created. Then it is a path that is traveled, a religion that is practiced, a doctrine that is chosen, and a belief that is held, and all of that is created. Then Allah Most High...
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage contains a refutation of those who deny that faith is created. First, the author asks the denier a probing question about the reality of faith—whether it is tasdiq, iqrar, deeds, etc.—forcing him to admit that it is one of these, all of which are created. Then he cites two hadiths: one where the Prophet says Allah created faith and surrounded it with generosity and modesty, and another that Allah created a hundred mercies, noting that faith is called mercy, so it must be among what is created. Moreover, faith has an opposite and a similar, which are characteristics of created things. Finally, faith is described as a path, religion, doctrine, and belief—all of which are created. The argument concludes that faith is undeniably created.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think faith is not created because it is a divine gift or an inner state that is not a created thing.
Correction: The passage shows that faith is created because it is composed of created acts (tasdiq, iqrar), it is explicitly mentioned as created in hadith, it has opposites and similarities (characteristics of created things), and it is described as a path, religion, etc., which are all created.

Checkpoint

A) Faith is mercy, so it is created
B) Mercy is not created
C) Faith is not mercy
D) Only a hundred mercies are created

Key Takeaway
Imam Maturidi proves that faith is created through logical questioning, hadith evidence, and the fact that it has opposites and is described as a created entity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If faith is created by Allah and surrounded by generosity and modesty, how will you embody those qualities in your interactions today as a deliberate act of faith?
?
The argument that faith has an opposite (disbelief) implies that your faith must be actively maintained. What concrete actions will you take this week to protect your faith from its opposite?
?
When the author asks the denier to define faith, he forces a personal examination. If you had to define your faith right now, would it be a passive label or an active, created reality in your heart and limbs? What would need to change for it to be the latter?

Maturidi on the Forgiveness of Major Sins
Maturidi states that major sins are forgivable and condemns Khawarij and Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's position that major sins are subject to possible divine forgiveness and that the Khawarij and Mu'tazila are mistaken in declaring grave sinners as eternal inhabitants of Hell.

Core Idea
Major sins are forgivable; only extremists claim otherwise.

Maturidi addresses a critical theological dispute: what happens to a Muslim who commits a major sin? The Khawarij and Mu'tazila held that such a person becomes a disbeliever deserving eternal punishment. Maturidi defends the mainstream Sunni view that forgiveness is possible, and that those who deny Allah's forgiveness are themselves misguided.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Maturidi's own words establish the principle before you interpret.
Notice the logical flow: from major sins to minor sins, then to the sectarian difference.
2
Identify the two key claims
The passage makes two main claims: (1) major sins are within the realm of possible forgiveness, and minor sins even more so; (2) the Khawarij and Mu'tazila who declare grave sinners as disbelievers are refuted, while believers who affirm God's mercy have hope.
These claims are connected: the first justifies the second.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: what does this passage tell us about God's mercy? How does Maturidi's view differ from the Khawarij and Mu'tazila?
This connects to the broader Sunni belief in hope and forgiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر ما قيل في الكبائر ، فإنها إذ صارت بحيث احتمال العفو ، فما دونها الصغائر - أولى ، وبما للقول به فيها على الاختلاف أثر بين في الأمة ، فصرف الكلام إليه أحق . ثم الحق أن يقال : جميع الخوارج والمعتزلة عند ارتكابهم الكبائر كفرة على قولهم ، مستوجبون للخلود في النار وأما المؤمنون بآيات الله وصفوه عفوا غفوراً رحيماً ، محققين لذلك ، فهم الذين لهم الرجاء.
Then we mention what has been said about the major sins (kabāʼir). For when they become such that forgiveness is possible for them, then what is below them—the minor sins (ṣaghāʼir)—is more deserving. And because the statement about them, despite the difference, has a clear impact among the nation, directing speech to it is more appropriate. Then the truth is to say: All the Khawarij and Mu'tazila, when they commit major sins, are disbelievers according to their own claim, deserving eternal abiding in the Fire. As for the believers in God's verses who describe Him as Oft-Forgiving, Most Merciful, affirming that, they are the ones who have hope.
Source pages p. 557

Commentary
This passage is a combination of two statements from Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid on the topic of major sins. The first statement establishes the principle that major sins are within the realm of possibility of forgiveness ('afw). Since major sins are subject to the possibility of divine pardon, then minor sins are even more deserving of forgiveness. Maturidi then notes that the difference of opinion on this matter has had a clear effect among the Muslim community, so it is important to address it. The second statement gives the ruling on those who hold extreme positions: the Khawarij and Mu'tazila, who according to their own teachings declare grave sinners as disbelievers deserving eternal punishment, are themselves refuted. Maturidi affirms that believers who truly affirm God's attributes of forgiveness and mercy are the ones who have hope for salvation. The passage thus defends the moderate Sunni position that major sins do not necessarily entail eternal punishment.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi equates all grave sinners with disbelievers, but he is actually refuting the Khawarij and Mu'tazila who hold that view.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly says the Khawarij and Mu'tazila are disbelievers 'according to their own claim', meaning they are wrong in that claim, not that all grave sinners are disbelievers.

Checkpoint

A) They are absolutely unforgivable.
B) They are subject to the possibility of forgiveness.
C) They automatically remove one from faith.
D) They are only forgivable after punishment in Hell.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi affirms that major sins do not necessarily lead to eternal punishment; only those who deny Allah's forgiveness are in error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that Allah is Oft-Forgiving, Most Merciful, but when you look at your own major sins, do you actually hope for His forgiveness, or do you despair? What would change in your daily life if you truly affirmed that forgiveness is possible?
?
The Khawarij and Mu'tazila judged others based on sins. Are there people you have written off as lost because of their sins? How does this passage challenge you to reconsider your attitude?
?
If you truly believe that minor sins are even more deserving of forgiveness than major ones, why do you still allow small sins to accumulate without turning to Allah? What would it look like to live with constant hope in His mercy?

Human Action as Kasb: Maturidi's Middle Path
Maturidi explains human actions as created by God but acquired by humans, avoiding extremes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's doctrine of kasb and how it preserves both divine creation and human free will.

Core Idea
Human actions are created by Allah but freely acquired by humans—kasb bridges divine power and human responsibility.

Maturidi was faced with two extreme views: the Jabriyya who denied human agency, and the Mu'tazila who denied divine involvement in human actions. He proposed a middle path called kasb, where actions are created by Allah but acquired by humans. This passage lays out that position clearly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then its translation. Notice Maturidi's strong opening claim that humans are true free agents.
This is a dense philosophical text—take your time.
2
Identify the Quranic evidence
The author cites four Quranic verses to support human freedom and responsibility. Find them and consider how they affirm choice.
The verses are: 'Do whatever you will,' 'Do good,' 'reward for what they used to do,' and 'Whoever does an atom's weight.'
3
Understand the attribution distribution
The passage explains that actions are attributed to Allah as creation (khalq) and to humans as acquisition (kasb). Note that one does not negate the other.
Key sentence: 'when attributed to Allah, it is creation; when attributed to the servant, it is called kasb.'
4
Contrast with the two extremes
Maturidi positions himself between Jabriyya (no human role) and Mu'tazila (no divine role). How does kasb avoid both?
Jabriyya make humans like machines; Mu'tazila deny Allah's effecting of actions.
5
Reflect on the middle path
Consider why this matter is important: it preserves God's sovereignty and human accountability. Summarize in your own words.
This doctrine is a hallmark of Maturidi theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإنسان فاعل مختار على الحقيقة ، فكل واحد منا يعلم من نفسه أنه مختار لا يفعله وأنه فاعل كاسب ، كما أن الله سبحانه وتعالى حقق للإنسان الفعل والاختيار فقال تعالى : اعْمَلُوا مَا شِنْتُم ، وقال : وافْعَلُوا الْخَيْرَ ، وقال : جَزَاءً بِمَا كَانُوا يَعْمَلُونَ ، وقال : فَمَنْ يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ ، وأفعال الإنسان وإن كانت كسباً له إلا أنها مخلوقة لله كما قال : واللهُ خَلَقَكُمْ وَمَا تَعْمَلُونَ . وليس في إضافتها إلى الله نفيها عن الإنسان بل هي الله بأن خلتها على ما عليه وأوجادها بعد أن لم تكن ، والخلق على ما كسبوها وفعلوها. وهكذا يتوزع الفعل عند الماتريدي بين الله وبين الإنسان ، إذا أضيف إلى الله أضيف إلى العبد يسمى كسباً . وبذلك يتوسل الماتريدي بين الجبرية التي سلبت الإنسان من كل فعل واختيار وسيرته كالآلة وبين المنزلة التي لم تجعل الله تا بيرا
And the human being is a true free agent, for each of us knows from himself that he is free and does not force himself, and that he is a doer and acquirer. Likewise, Allah, glorified and exalted, actualized for the human being action and choice. He said: 'Do whatever you will,' and 'Do good,' and 'As a reward for what they used to do,' and 'Whoever does an atom's weight [of good].' And human actions, even though they are acquisition for him, are created by Allah, as He said: 'And Allah created you and what you do.' There is no negation of the action from the human by attributing it to Allah; rather, it is [attributed] to Allah by His creating it as it is and bringing it into existence after it was not, and the creation is according to what they have acquired and done. Thus, action according to Maturidi is distributed between Allah and the human: when attributed to Allah, it is creation (khalq); when attributed to the servant, it is called acquisition (kasb). By this, Maturidi takes a middle path between the Jabriyya, who stripped the human of all action and choice and made him like a machine, and the Mu'tazila, who did not make Allah an effecter [in human actions].
Source pages p. 582

Commentary
This passage is a summary of Maturidi's doctrine on human action (kasb). The author begins by asserting that the human being is a true free agent (fa'il mukhtar 'ala al-haqiqa) based on self-awareness: each person knows that he is free and not compelled. Allah Himself has confirmed this by commanding action and choice in the Quran. However, these actions are also created by Allah, as stated in the verse 'Allah created you and what you do.' Attributing actions to Allah does not negate their attribution to humans; rather, creation (khalq) is from Allah, while acquisition (kasb) is from the human. The author then clarifies Maturidi's position as a middle path between two extremes: the Jabriyya (fatalists) who deny human agency entirely, and the Mu'tazila who deny any role for Allah in human actions. Maturidi's concept of kasb allows both: actions are created by Allah but are freely acquired by humans, thereby preserving divine sovereignty and human responsibility.

Key Terms
كسب
فعل
اختيار
جبر
خلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi's kasb means God compels actions, leaving no room for human choice.
Correction: Kasb affirms that while God creates the action, the human freely chooses and acquires it, making the human truly responsible.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction Maturidi makes to avoid both Jabriyya and Mu'tazila extremes?
A) Actions are partially created by God and partially by humans.
B) Actions are created by God but acquired by humans, called kasb.
C) Actions are purely from human free will, and God approves them.
D) Actions are illusions; only God acts.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's kasb doctrine affirms that human actions are truly created by Allah and truly chosen by humans, avoiding both fatalism and rationalist denial of divine intervention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a choice, do you truly feel it is your own, or do you sometimes act as if you are a machine following a script? How does Maturidi's teaching of kasb challenge you to take full responsibility for your actions while recognizing Allah's creation of them?
?
What if the 'middle path' of kasb is not a comfortable compromise but a call to live in constant awareness of Allah's sovereignty without ever using it as an excuse for sin? Where in your life have you hidden behind 'it was written' to avoid accountability?
?
The Quran commands 'Do whatever you will'—if you truly believed that each action is both created by Allah and chosen by you, would it change the way you approach small, everyday decisions? What one habit would you start choosing differently today?

Maturidi's Doctrine of Kasb: Man as True Agent
Maturidi argues that man is a true agent and actions are created by Allah, defining kasb as the human's acquisition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's middle position on human action: that man is a true free agent whose actions are created by Allah but acquired (kasb) by the human, and how this mediates between Jabriyya and Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
Man truly acts, but Allah creates; we acquire (kasb) our deeds.

This lesson presents Maturidi's key doctrine of kasb (acquisition), which explains how human actions can be both truly ours and entirely created by Allah. The passage you will read from Imam al-Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid sets out the evidence from self-knowledge and the Quran, and shows how this positions him between two extreme sects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Let the author's own words set the foundation.
Notice the Quranic citations—they are central to the argument.
2
Identify the two claims Maturidi establishes
Find where Maturidi asserts that man is a true free agent, and then where he says actions are created by Allah. How does he support each claim?
He uses both self-knowledge (each of us knows we are free) and Quranic verses.
3
Understand the concept of kasb (acquisition)
Focus on the sentence that explains the distribution of action: when attributed to Allah it is creation, when to the servant it is acquisition. Grasp that kasb means the human 'acquires' the act that Allah creates.
Kasb is not 'creating' but 'earning' the act.
4
See the mediation between extremes
Maturidi says his view mediates between Jabriyya (who deny human agency) and the Mu'tazila (who deny Allah's creation of actions). Reflect on how kasb avoids both errors.
The passage becomes corrupted at the very end but the main point is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإنسان فاعل مختار على الحقيقة ، فكل واحد منا يعلم من نفسه أنه مختار لا يفعله وأنه فاعل كاسب ، كما أن الله سبحانه وتعالى حقق للإنسان الفعل والاختيار فقال تعالى : اعْمَلُوا مَا شِنْتُم ، وقال : " وافْعَلُوا الْخَيْرَ ، وقال : ، ، جَزَاءً بِمَا كَانُوا يَعْمَلُونَ ، وقال : فَمَنْ يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ ، وأفعال الإنسان وإن كانت كسباً له إلا أنها مخلوقة لله كما قال : واللهُ خَلَقَكُمْ وَمَا تَعْمَلُونَ " . وليس في إضافتها إلى الله نفيها عن الإنسان بل هي الله بأن خلتها على ما عليه وأوجادها بعد أن لم تكن ، والخلق على ما كسبوها وفعلوها ٧٠ . وهكذا يتوزع الفعل عند الماتريدي بين الله وبين الإنسان ، إذا أضيف إلى الله أضيف إلى العبد يسمى كسباً . وبذلك يتوسل الماتريدي بين الجبرية التي سلبت الإنسان من كل فعل واختيار وسيرته كالآلة وبين المنزلة التي لم تجعل الله تا بيرا
And man is a true free agent, for each one of us knows from himself that he is free and does not [just] do it, and that he is an agent and acquirer. Just as Allah the Exalted has realized for man the act and choice, saying: 'Do whatever you wish'; and said: 'And do good'; and said: 'As a recompense for what they used to do'; and said: 'So whoever does an atom's weight [of good]'. And the actions of man, even though they are his acquisition, are nevertheless created by Allah, as He said: 'And Allah created you and what you do.' And in attributing them to Allah there is no negation of them from man; rather, Allah brings them into existence as they are and originates them after they were not, and the creation is according to what they have acquired and done. Thus action is distributed in al-Maturidi's view between Allah and man: when attributed to Allah it is creation, and when attributed to the servant it is called acquisition (kasb). By this, al-Maturidi mediates between the Jabriyya who stripped man of all action and choice and made him like a tool, and between the [Mu'tazila] who did not make Allah a creator...
Source pages p. 582

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's doctrine of human action, affirming that man is truly a free agent. The passage begins by stating that each person knows from himself that he is free and an acquirer. It then cites Quranic verses that command action and reward deeds, proving that humans have real choice and action. The passage also cites verses that show actions are created by Allah. It explains that attributing actions to Allah does not negate their attribution to man; Allah creates the actions according to what humans acquire and do. Then it summarizes Maturidi's middle position: action is creation (khalq) when attributed to Allah, and acquisition (kasb) when attributed to the servant. This positions Maturidi between the Jabriyya (who deny human agency) and the Mu'tazila (who deny Allah's creation of actions). The final part mentions 'the one who did not make Allah a creator' likely referring to the Mu'tazila. The passage ends abruptly with 'ta bayyara', probably 'tabayyana' or similar. Overall, the passage defines Maturidi's kasb theory.

Key Terms
الفعل الاختياري (voluntary act)
الكسب (acquisition/kasb)
الجبرية (Jabriyya)
المعتزلة (Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi's kasb means that humans create their own actions.
Correction: Actually, kasb means that humans 'acquire' actions that are created by Allah. Humans do not bring actions into existence from nothing; they choose and earn the act that Allah creates.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the position that Maturidi's doctrine of kasb mediates between?
A) Jabriyya and Mu'tazila
B) Mu'tazila and Ash'ariyya
C) Jabriyya and Qadariyya
D) Salaf and Khalaf

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's kasb affirms that humans are real agents whose actions are entirely created by Allah, balancing divine omnipotence and human responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as though every action you choose is created by Allah, yet you are fully accountable? How does that change the way you approach your next sin or good deed?
?
When you fail to do good, do you blame your circumstances or your own choice? How can you take full responsibility today, knowing you are a real agent?
?
If you truly believed your actions are your acquisition, would you make the same excuses for your shortcomings? What would you do differently starting now?

Maturidi's Definition of Justice and Proof from Two Aspects
Maturidi defines justice and gives a two-aspect proof for human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's definition of justice (adl) and his proof that human actions have two aspects—one from Allah and one from the servant.

Core Idea
Justice means affirming both Allah's sovereignty and human accountability.

Maturidi opens his discussion of divine justice by defining it as the affirmation of both Allah’s description as Creator and Powerful, and His justice and grace. He then provides a proof from the nature of human actions, showing that each action has an aspect that only Allah can create (existence) and an aspect that the servant intends and chooses (movement within commands).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice Maturidi's method of starting with definition then proof.
The Arabic contains a repetition that may be a scribal error; focus on the meaning.
2
Identify the definition of justice
Find the two parts of the definition: (1) affirming Allah's attributes as Creator and Powerful, (2) affirming His justice and grace. This sets the foundation.
3
Understand the two-aspect proof
Trace the logic: actions have states beyond human reach (existence from nothing) and states within human capacity (specific movements). From the first aspect, actions are not from the servant; from the second, they are.
This proof supports the kasb theory of acquisition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول الماتريدي : والعدل هو القول بتحقيق الأمرين ؛ ليكون الله موصوفاً بما وصف به نفسه محموداً به كما قال : خَالِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ ، وقال : فَهُوَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَ شَيْءٍ قَدِيرٌ ، وليكون عدلا متفضلاً كما قال : وَمَا رَبُّكَ بِظلام لِلْعَبِيدِ وقال : وَلَوْلا فَضلُ اللَّهِ عَلَيْكُمْ وَرَحْمَتُهُ لاتَّبَعْتُمُ الشَّيْطَانَ إِلا قَلِيلا ، ثم الدليل على لزوم القول بهذا - مع ما فيما بينا كفاية ما فيما بينا كفاية - وجود أحوال في أفعال العبد لا يبلغها أوهامهم ولا يقدرها عقولهم ، وأحوال فيها ينتهي إليها قصدهم وتبلغها عقولهم قثبت أنها من الوجه الأول ليست لهم ، ومن الوجه الثاني لهم . فالأول كتصوير خروج الشيء من العدم إلى الوجود ... والثاني نحو التحرك والسكون بالمنهى والمأمور به ، ثبت أن فعلهم من الوجه الأول ليس لهم ، ومن الثاني لهم.
Al-Maturidi says: 'Adl (justice) is the affirmation of both matters: so that Allah is described with what He described Himself with, praised thereby, as He said: 'Creator of everything', and said: 'He over everything is powerful'; and so that He is just and gracious, as He said: 'And your Lord is not unjust to His servants', and said: 'And were it not for the bounty of Allah upon you and His mercy, you would have followed Satan, except a few.' Then the proof for the necessity of holding this view—with what we have already explained being sufficient—is the existence of states in the actions of the servant that their imaginations do not reach nor their minds comprehend, and states that their intention reaches and their minds attain. So it is established that from the first aspect they are not from them, and from the second aspect they are from them. The first is like the formation of a thing's emergence from non-existence into existence... and the second is like movement and stillness regarding what is forbidden and commanded. Thus it is established that their action from the first aspect is not from them, and from the second is from them.
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage contains a direct quotation from Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid defining justice (adl) and providing a proof. It begins by stating that justice is the affirmation of both matters: that Allah is described as the Creator and Powerful, and that Allah is just and gracious. Then Maturidi gives the proof: there are aspects of the servant's actions that their minds cannot grasp (the coming into existence from nothing), and aspects that their intentions and minds can reach (the specific movement or stillness in obedience or sin). From the first aspect, the action is not from the servant; from the second, it is from them. This proof establishes the two-aspect nature of human acts, supporting the kasb theory.

Key Terms
العدل
الوجه الأول
الوجه الثاني
خروج الشيء من العدم إلى الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof is often misunderstood as endorsing full free will (libertarian free will).
Correction: The passage shows that human agency is limited to the specific 'how' of an action (e.g., movement), not its existence. The action's existence is from Allah alone.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first aspect of human actions that is not from the servant?
A) Movement and stillness
B) Intention and purpose
C) The emergence from non-existence to existence
D) Obedience and disobedience

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's concept of justice requires affirming that human actions have an aspect from Allah (existence) and an aspect from the servant (specific choice), preserving both divine sovereignty and human accountability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you ever feel pride as if you originated it yourself? How does Maturidi's two-aspect view strip you of that pride and redirect gratitude to Allah alone?
?
Consider a sin you struggle with. Do you tend to excuse yourself by saying 'Allah created my actions' or blame yourself entirely? How does the two-aspect teaching expose the error in both extremes and call you to both humility and repentance?
?
If your very ability to move or choose is from Allah, what does that mean for the intentions behind your daily actions? Will you begin each action today by acknowledging that its existence is from Him and its specific course is your responsibility?

Intention as the Basis for Reward and Punishment
Maturidi explains intention as the basis for moral judgment and presents two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi identifies intention (qasd) as the basis for moral responsibility and reward/punishment, and how this relates to the divine creation of power at the moment of intent.

Core Idea
Reward and punishment depend on intention, not the action itself.

Imam Maturidi now addresses a key question in Islamic theology: what makes an action worthy of reward or punishment? He argues that the act itself is neutral; it is the intention that determines moral value. This passage establishes the role of human intention (qasd) in the context of divine power (qudra), and provides evidence from revelation and reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Maturidi begins by describing how voluntary actions occur, then moves to the basis of moral judgment.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main claim
The central claim is that reward and punishment are founded on intention (qasd), not the act itself. Find where this is stated.
Look for the phrase 'فالفعل في ذاته ليس مناط الثواب والعقاب'.
3
Analyze the two proofs
Maturidi gives two evidences: the hadith about the pen being raised from three, and the rational argument that an act can be good or evil depending on intention. Compare how each supports the main claim.
The second proof uses an example 'كالمجرة' which may be unclear; focus on the logical structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتقع أفعال الإنسان الاختيارية بقدرة أو استطاعة يخلقها الله في الإنسان عند قصد اكتساب الفعل بعد سلامة الأسباب والآلات . فعند قصد فعل ما من الأفعال توفر أسبابه وآلاته يخلق الله تعالى قدرة هذا الفعل. واستحقاق الذم والعقاب أو المدح والثواب على فعل ما من الأفعال مؤسس على هذا القصد ؛ لأن الإنسان عندما يقصد فعل الشر فيخلق الله له قدرة فعل الشر يكون هو المضيع لقدرة فعل الخير بعدم القصد إليه ، وعندما يقصد فعل الخير يخلق الله له قدرة فعل الخير ويستحق المدح والثواب على قصده الخير فالفعل في ذاته ليس مناط الثواب والعقاب ، وإنما الثواب والعقاب على الفعل باعتبار القصد إليه ؛ يدل على ذلك أن الفعل من غير قصد إليه كفعل النائم والمجنون لا يؤسس عليه ثواب أو عقاب لقوله عليه السلام رفع القلم عن ثلاثة : عن النائم حتى يستيقظ وعن الصبي حتى يحتلم وعن المجنون حتى يعقل . ويدل على ذلك أيضاً أن الفعل في ذاته قد يكون صالحا للخير والشر كالمجرة مثلاً ، فهى في ذاتها لا يتوجه إليها ثواب أو عقاب إنما يتوجه إليها باعتبار القصد لقوله عليه السلام وإنما الأعمال بالنيات وإنما لكل
And the voluntary actions of man occur by a power (qudra) or capacity (istita'a) that Allah creates in man at the intention (qasd) of acquiring the action after the soundness of causes and instruments. So when intending a particular action, its causes and instruments being available, Allah creates the power for that action. And the deserving of blame and punishment or praise and reward for an action is founded on this intention; because when man intends evil, Allah creates for him the power for evil, and he becomes the one who wasted the power for good by not intending it. And when he intends good, Allah creates for him the power for good, and he deserves praise and reward for his intention of good. So the action in itself is not the basis of reward and punishment; rather, reward and punishment are on the action by virtue of the intention towards it. Evidence for that is that an action without intention, like the action of a sleeping person or a mad person, does not found any reward or punishment, due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'The pen is raised from three: from the sleeper until he wakes, from the child until he reaches puberty, and from the insane until he recovers reason.' And evidence for that also is that the action in itself may be suitable for good and evil, like the qadha? for instance, so in itself no reward or punishment is directed to it; rather it is directed to it by virtue of intention, due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Actions are but by intentions, and for each...'
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage explains the mechanics of Maturidi's kasb theory with emphasis on intention (qasd). It begins by stating that voluntary human actions occur by a power that Allah creates in man at the moment of intending the action, after the instruments are sound. So when one intends an act, Allah creates the power for that specific act. The passage then argues that reward and punishment are based on this intention. If one intends evil, Allah creates the power for evil, and the person wastes the power for good; if one intends good, Allah creates the power for good, and the person deserves reward. Thus the locus of reward/punishment is the intention, not the act itself. Two proofs are given: first, the hadith about the three from whom the pen is raised (sleeper, child, insane), showing that actions without intention carry no judgment. Second, a rational argument: an act in itself may be good or evil depending on context (like qadha? perhaps a typo for 'sadaqa' or 'zina'?), so the judgment is based on intention. The passage ends with the famous hadith 'Actions are but by intentions.' This clarifies the role of qasd in moral responsibility.

Key Terms
القصد (intention/qasd)
القدرة (qudra/power)
الاستطاعة (istita'a/capacity)
رفع القلم (the pen is raised)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that human power (qudra) exists independently before intention, or that reward/punishment are based solely on the act itself.
Correction: The passage clarifies that Allah creates the power at the moment of intention, and moral judgment is based on intention, not the act.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for deserving reward or punishment in actions?
A) The act itself
B) The intention (qasd)
C) The created power
D) The soundness of instruments

Key Takeaway
Intention (qasd) is the decisive factor for moral responsibility; Allah creates the power for action at the moment of intention, and reward/punishment follow the intention, not the act itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you intend something you know displeases Allah, do you realize that at that very moment Allah creates the power for that action, and you become responsible for that intention? What does this teach you about the weight of your secret thoughts? Take a moment to repent for an intention you harbored today.
?
The hadith about the pen being lifted from the sleeper, child, and insane reminds us that accountability requires conscious intention. Are there areas in your life where you pretend to be 'asleep'—not fully aware or responsible—to avoid accountability? Name one specific area and commit to waking up to it starting now.
?
Actions are judged by intentions. If Allah were to weigh your prayers and daily deeds solely by the sincerity of your intention, would you be satisfied? What one change will you make today to purify your intention before your next action?

Human Actions, Capacity, and Intention
Explanation of voluntary action and intention-based moral accountability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi view that voluntary human actions are performed with a power created by Allah at the moment of intention, and that reward and punishment are based on intention, not the act itself.

Core Idea
Reward and punishment are tied to your intention, not the action itself.

Imam Maturidi explains how human actions happen and what makes them worthy of reward or punishment. He argues that Allah creates an enabling power at the moment you intend an action, and it is your intention that determines moral responsibility. Two prophetic hadiths support this: one about actions without intention (sleep, childhood, insanity) and the famous hadith that actions are judged by intentions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Pay attention to the key terms: qasd (intention), istita'a (capacity), and qudra haditha (created power).
The passage uses technical kalam vocabulary.
2
Understand the mechanism of action
Notice how Maturidi describes the creation of power: Allah creates power at the moment of intention, provided the causes and instruments are sound. This is the Maturidi 'kasb' (acquisition) theory in action.
3
Identify the role of intention
Focus on the sentence: 'Thus the act itself is not the basis for reward and punishment; rather reward and punishment are on the act by virtue of intention.' This is the core claim.
Contrast this with views that make the act itself inherently good or evil.
4
Examine the two prophetic proofs
Read the two hadiths: (1) the lifting of the pen from three categories, and (2) the 'actions are but by intentions' hadith. See how each one supports Maturidi's claim that intention is necessary for moral accountability.
The second hadith is from Sahih Bukhari and Muslim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتقع أفعال الإنسان الاختيارية بقدرة أو استطاعة يخلقها الله في الإنسان عند قصد اكتساب الفعل بعد سلامة الأسباب والآلات. فعند قصد فعل ما من الأفعال توفر أسبابه وآلاته يخلق الله تعالى قدرة هذا الفعل. واستحقاق الذم والعقاب أو المدح والثواب على فعل ما من الأفعال مؤسس على هذا القصد؛ لأن الإنسان عندما يقصد فعل الشر فيخلق الله له قدرة فعل الشر يكون هو المضيع لقدرة فعل الخير بعدم القصد إليه، وعندما يقصد فعل الخير يخلق الله له قدرة فعل الخير ويستحق المدح والثواب على قصده الخير فالفعل في ذاته ليس مناط الثواب والعقاب، وإنما الثواب والعقاب على الفعل باعتبار القصد إليه؛ يدل على ذلك أن الفعل من غير قصد إليه كفعل النائم والمجنون لا يؤسس عليه ثواب أو عقاب لقوله عليه السلام رفع القلم عن ثلاثة: عن النائم حتى يستيقظ وعن الصبي حتى يحتلم وعن المجنون حتى يعقل. ويدل على ذلك أيضاً أن الفعل في ذاته قد يكون صالحا للخير والشر كالمجرة مثلاً، فهى في ذاتها لا يتوجه إليها ثواب أو عقاب إنما يتوجه إليها باعتبار القصد لقوله عليه السلام وإنما الأعمال بالنيات وإنما لكل امرئ ما نوى، فمن كانت هجرته إلى الله ورسوله فهجرته إلى الله ورسوله، ومن كانت هجرته إلى دنيا يصيبها أو امرأة ينكحها فهجرته إلى ما هاجر إليه.
Voluntary human actions occur by a power or capacity that Allah creates in the human at the intention of acquiring the act after the safety of causes and instruments. So when one intends a particular action, its causes and instruments are available, Allah creates the power for that action. And entitlement to blame and punishment or praise and reward for an action is founded on this intention; because when a person intends evil, Allah creates for him the power for evil, and he becomes the one who wastes the power for good by not intending it, and when he intends good, Allah creates for him the power for good and he deserves praise and reward for his intention of good. Thus the act itself is not the basis for reward and punishment; rather reward and punishment are on the act by virtue of intention. Evidence for that is that an act without intention, like the act of a sleeper or an insane person, does not incur reward or punishment, due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'The pen is lifted from three: from the sleeper until he wakes, from the child until he reaches puberty, and from the insane until he recovers reason.' And further evidence is that the act itself may be suitable for good or evil, like the hijra [or: galaxy] for example; it in itself does not attract reward or punishment, but rather attracts it by virtue of intention, due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'Actions are but by intentions, and each person will have only what he intended. So whoever's emigration was for Allah and His Messenger, his emigration was for Allah and His Messenger; and whoever's emigration was for a worldly gain or a woman to marry, his emigration was for that which he emigrated.'
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage explains the mechanism of voluntary human actions according to Maturidi. Actions occur when God creates a power or capacity in the human at the moment of intending an act, after the necessary causes and instruments are present. The key point is that reward and punishment are based on intention, not the act itself. If someone intends evil, God creates the power for that evil action, and the person is blameworthy because they wasted the potential for good by not intending it. Similarly for good. The act itself is neutral; its moral value comes from intention. Two proofs are given: first, the hadith that the pen is lifted from three categories (sleeper, child, insane) who lack intention, so their actions incur no reward/punishment. Second, the example of an act that can be either good or bad depending on intention, like hijra (emigration) which could be for Allah or for worldly gain, as established in the famous hadith. Thus, Maturidi grounds moral accountability in the human's intention (qasd), not in the created act.

Key Terms
قصد
استطاعة
قدرة حادثة
رفع القلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That reward and punishment are based on the act itself, regardless of intention.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that the act itself is neutral; moral accountability depends on the intention. Even if Allah creates the power for the act, the human's intention determines blame or praise.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, on what basis are reward and punishment given?
A) The act itself, regardless of intention
B) The intention behind the act
C) The power created by God
D) The availability of instruments

Key Takeaway
In Maturidi theology, moral accountability depends on human intention (qasd), even though Allah creates the power to act at the moment of intention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider your last five good deeds. Can you honestly say they were all done with the sole intention of pleasing Allah, or were some tainted by seeking praise or worldly gain? What does the hijra example teach you about the danger of mixing intentions, and how will you guard against it starting tomorrow?
?
The hadith of the lifted pen shows that only those with sound intention are accountable. You are accountable right now. If your intention in seeking knowledge today is not for Allah, what then is the value of your study? Pause and correct your intention before you continue.
?
You have the power to intend good or evil. But Allah creates the power to act only after your intention. Does your pattern of intentions reflect a heart that desires good or one that constantly wastes the opportunity for good by intending evil? What is one concrete change you need to make in your intentions today?

Maturidi's Twofold Division of Capacity
Maturidi divides capacity into safety of causes and accompanying power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's distinction between capacity as safety of causes (precondition for action) and capacity as the originated power that accompanies the action.

Core Idea
Capacity has two meanings: preconditions for action and the power that makes action happen.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi clarifies a common confusion about the term 'capacity' (istita'a). He shows that the capacity required for religious obligations like Hajj is the safety of means and instruments—not a separate power created at the moment of action. Then he outlines his own principle: power (qudra) is of two kinds—one that precedes the act and one that accompanies it.

Guided Path
1
Understand the everyday meaning of istita'a
Read the first part of the passage where Maturidi explains that istita'a is often used to mean having the necessary tools and sound limbs, not an internal power. For example, being capable of writing requires a pen, paper, and a working hand.
This shows that capacity in common usage refers to external conditions.
2
Examine the Quranic example
Look at how the Quranic verse about Hajj uses istita'a: it means having provision and a mount. Maturidi says this is the basis for obligation (taklif).
The obligation depends on this kind of capacity, not on the originated power.
3
Learn Maturidi's twofold division
Read Maturidi's own statement dividing power into two: (1) safety of causes and soundness of instruments, which precede the action and are a grace from Allah, and (2) a second meaning that is tied to the action itself and cannot exist without it.
The second type is the effective power that God creates at the moment of action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تطلق الاستطاعة ويراد بها سلامة الأسباب والآلات والجوارح، لا القدرة الحادثة. فيقال مثلاً هذا الشخص مستطيع للكتابة إذا كان معه قلم وورقة وكانت يده سليمة، ويقال هو عاجز عن الكتابة إذا كان لا يجد قلماً ولا ورقاً أو وجد القلم والورق وكانت يده شلاء، وبهذا - أي بسلامة الأسباب والآلات والجوارح تفسر الاستطاعة في قوله تعالى: وَلِلَّهِ عَلَى النَّاسِ حِجُّ البَيْتِ مَنِ اسْتَطَاعَ إِلَيْهِ سَبِيلاً ومعلوم أنه لا سبيل إلى ذلك إلا بالزاد والراحلة. والتكليف يعتمد على الاستطاعة بهذا المعنى الذي هو سلامة الأسباب والآلات والجوارح دون المعنى الأول وهو القدرة الحادثة. ولا شك أن الاستطاعة بهذا المعنى متقدمة على الفعل. يقول الماتريدي: الأصل عندنا في المسمى باسم القدرة أنها على قسمين: سلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات وهي تتقدم الأفعال، وحقيقتها ليست للأفعال وإن كانت الأفعال لا تقوم إلا بها، لكنها نعمة من الله أكرم بها من هو معه... والثاني معنى لا يقدر على تبين حده بشيء يصار إليه سوى للفعل، لا يجوز وجوده بحال إلا ويقع به الفعل عندما يقع.
And the term 'capacity' (istitaʿa) is sometimes used to mean safety of causes, instruments, and limbs, not the originated power. For example, it is said: this person is capable of writing if he has a pen and paper and his hand is sound, and he is said to be incapable of writing if he finds no pen or paper or finds pen and paper but his hand is paralyzed. And by this—that is, by safety of causes, instruments, and limbs—is interpreted the capacity in His saying: 'And to Allah belongs the pilgrimage to the House for whoever has the capacity to find a way there' and it is known that there is no way to that except with provision and mount. And the obligation (taklif) depends on capacity in this sense, which is safety of causes, instruments, and limbs, not the first sense which is the originated power. And there is no doubt that capacity in this sense precedes the action. Al-Maturidi says: The principle with us regarding what is called 'power' (qudra) is that it has two divisions: safety of causes and soundness of instruments, which precede actions, and their reality is not for actions even though actions are not established without them, but they are a grace from Allah with which He honors whomever He wills... And the second is a meaning that cannot be defined by something that can be resorted to except for the action; it cannot exist in any case except that the action occurs by it when it occurs.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage deals with the meaning of 'capacity' (istitaʿa) and its two senses. First, it clarifies that capacity often refers to the safety of causes, instruments, and limbs—like having a pen, paper, and a working hand for writing. This is the sense used in the Quranic verse about Hajj: it means having provision and a mount. This kind of capacity precedes the action and is the basis for obligation (taklif). Second, there is another sense: the originated power (al-qudra al-haditha) that God creates at the moment of action. Maturidi then quotes his own principle: power (qudra) is divided into two: (1) safety of causes and soundness of instruments, which precede actions and are a grace from God, and (2) a meaning that cannot be defined except in relation to the action, and it cannot exist without the action occurring with it. This second power accompanies the act. Maturidi thus distinguishes between the capacity that is a precondition (safety of causes) and the effective power that causes the action.

Key Terms
استطاعة
سلامة الأسباب
قدرة حادثة
قدرة متقدمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the capacity mentioned in the Quran (e.g., for Hajj) refers to the originated power created at the moment of action.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that the capacity in the Quran means safety of causes, instruments, and limbs—preconditions for action, not the power that causes it.

Checkpoint

A) The effective power that accompanies the act
B) Safety of causes and soundness of instruments
