Key Takeaway
Maturidi divides capacity into two: safety of causes (precondition) and the effective power that causes the action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your abilities—your health, your resources, your education—do you ever attribute them solely to your own effort? How does Maturidi's classification challenge you to see these as nothing but a grace from Allah that precedes your actions, not your own doing? Will you now consciously thank Him for the smallest means, like the pen in your hand, before you even use it?
?
Reflect on a recent good deed you performed. Which of the two senses of capacity did you rely on more: the external conditions (safety of causes) or the inner power that actually made you act? If you feel proud of your inner resolve, does Maturidi's point that the second type 'cannot exist without the action occurring' remind you that even that resolve is an originated power from Allah at the moment of action? How can you cultivate a constant awareness of His immediate sovereignty over every action you take?
?
Maturidi says that the second type of capacity 'لا يجوز وجوده بحال إلا ويقع به الفعل'—it cannot exist except when the action occurs. This means if you have the power to do something, you will do it; if you don't do it, you never had that power. Is there a good deed you keep postponing, telling yourself you have the power but just haven't acted? What does this passage reveal about that excuse? What step will you take today to stop deceiving yourself and actually act?

Istita'a as Safety of Means and Instruments
Defines istita'a as safety of causes, instruments, and limbs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term istita'a (capability) can refer to the safety of external means and physical soundness, distinct from the originated power that accompanies the act, and that this meaning is used in the Quranic verse on Hajj.

Core Idea
Istita'a means having the means, not the power to act.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi clarifies a key term in Islamic theology: istita'a. He distinguishes between two meanings of capability: the safety of causes, instruments, and limbs, and the originated power that accompanies the act. This lesson unpacks his definition and its application to the obligation of Hajj.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English translation carefully. Notice how the author begins with a definition and then provides an example.
2
Identify the two meanings of istita'a

3
Analyze the example of writing
Consider the example of writing: what makes someone capable of writing? How does this illustrate the concept of safety of means?
4
Connect to the Quranic verse
Read how Imam Maturidi uses the Hajj verse to support this definition. Why does he say obligation depends on inference?

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تطلق الاستطاعة ويراد بها سلامة الأسباب والآلات والجوارح، لا القدرة الحادثة. فيقال مثلاً هذا الشخص مستطيع للكتابة إذا كان معه قلم وورقة وكانت يده سليمة، ويقال هو عاجز عن الكتابة إذا كان لا يجد قلماً ولا ورقاً أو وجد القلم والورق وكانت يده شلاء، وبهذا - أي بسلامة الأسباب والآلات والجوارح تفسر الاستطاعة في قوله تعالى: (وَلِلَّهِ عَلَى النَّاسِ حِجُّ الْبَيْتِ مَنِ اسْتَطَاعَ إِلَيْهِ سَبِيلًا) ومعلوم أنه لا سبيل إلى ذلك إلا بالزاد والراحلة. والتكليف يعتمد على الاستدلال. بهذا المعنى الذي هو سلامة الأسباب والآلات والجوارح دون المعنى الأول لأن القدرة الحادثة. ولا شك أن الاستطاعة بهذا المعنى متقدمة على الفعل.
And the term capability (istita'a) is sometimes used to mean the safety of causes, instruments, and limbs, not the originated power (al-qudra al-haditha). For example, it is said: this person is capable of writing if he has a pen and paper and his hand is sound, and he is said to be incapable of writing if he does not find a pen and paper or if he finds the pen and paper but his hand is paralyzed. And by this—i.e., by safety of causes, instruments, and limbs—the capability is interpreted in the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'And to Allah is the pilgrimage to the House for whoever is able to find a way to it' and it is known that there is no way to that except with provision and mount. And obligation depends on inference. By this meaning, which is safety of causes, instruments, and limbs, not the first meaning, because the originated power. And there is no doubt that capability in this sense precedes the act.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage defines istita'a (capability) as the safety of external means and bodily soundness, distinguishing it from the originated power that accompanies the act. It provides an example of writing to illustrate, and cites the Quranic verse on Hajj to show that this type of capability is what is meant in the verse. The key point is that this capability precedes the act and is a condition for obligation (taklif).

Maturidi's Two Types of Power: Safety of Causes and Facilitated Power
Maturidi divides power into safety of causes and the actual power that accompanies the act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's distinction between the power that precedes acts (safety of causes) and the power that accompanies the act (facilitated power), and how this differs from the Mu'tazilite view.

Core Idea
Human power is twofold: the potential means and the actual act-enabling gift from Allah.

This passage from Imam Maturidi presents a nuanced understanding of human capacity. He distinguishes between the external conditions that enable action and the actual power that occurs simultaneously with the act. This division is key to Maturidi's position on divine predestination and human responsibility, contrasting with the Mu'tazilite claim that power precedes the act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse every word. Notice the two types of power introduced.
The Arabic has some unclear sections due to OCR, but the main division is clear.
2
Identify the first type: safety of causes
Find where Maturidi says 'safety of causes and soundness of instruments.' Note that this type precedes acts and is necessary but not sufficient.
3
Identify the second type: facilitated power
Find the second type described as 'facilitated power' (qudra muyassara). Notice that it is simultaneous with the act, not before.
This is the actual power that enables the act at the moment it occurs.
4
Compare with the Mu'tazilite view
The passage explicitly contrasts with the Mu'tazila who held that power precedes the act. Reflect on the theological implications.
Maturidi's view emphasizes divine involvement at the moment of action.
5
Reflect on the overall message
Consider how this division supports Maturidi's middle position between Qadariyya and Jabriyya affirm human responsibility while preserving divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول الماتريدي: الأصل عندنا في المسمى باسم القدرة أنها على قسمين: سلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات وهي تتقدم الأفعال، وحقيقتها ليست [؟] للأفعال وإن كانت الأفعال لا تقوم إلا بها، لكنها نعمة من الله أكرم بها من معه [؟] أحادها [؟] أنه ليس ليس إلا [؟] شاء [؟] والثاني معنى لا يقدر على تبين حده بشيء يصار إليه سوى للفعل، لا يجوز وجوده بحال إلا ويقع به الفعل عندما يقع إذن فالماتريدي يقسم القدرة التي هي مناط التكليف قسمين: 1) قدرة ما يسميها بسلامة الآلات وصحة الأسباب. وهذا تفسير للقارة باللازم؛ لأن القدرة حقيقة صفة يتأتي بها الفعل والترك وفق مشيئته. 2) قدرة ميسرة زائدة على القدرة الممكنة وهي التي يقدر الإنسان بها على الفعل [؟] ممكنة [؟] وهي تفضلاً من صحة أسباب اليسر هذه أيضاً الله تعالى: ولا بد مع [؟] من [؟] يه مع يسر والقدرة الميسرة مع الفعل عند الماتريدي لا قبلة خلافاً للمعتزلة ويخاهقا الله مقارنة
Maturidi says: The principle with us regarding that which is named 'power' (qudra) is that it is of two types: safety of causes and soundness of instruments, and it precedes acts; its reality is not [؟] for acts, even though acts are not established except through it, but it is a grace from Allah with which He honors whom He wills [؟] ... and the second is a meaning that cannot be defined by anything other than the act; it is not valid for it to exist in any case except that the act occurs by it when it occurs. Thus Maturidi divides the power which is the basis of obligation into two types: 1) a power that he calls safety of instruments and soundness of causes. This is an interpretation of power by its necessary concomitant; because power is truly an attribute by which the act and omission occur according to one's will. 2) a facilitated power (qudra muyassara) additional to the possible power, which is that by which man is able to act [؟] possible [؟] and it is a favor from the soundness of the causes of ease these also Allah the Exalted: and there must be with [؟] ... and the facilitated power with the act according to Maturidi is not prior, contrary to the Mu'tazila, and [؟].
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's classification of human power. The first type is the safety of external means, which precedes the act and is necessary but not sufficient. The second type is the actual power that accompanies the act, which is a gift from Allah. Maturidi asserts that this second power is simultaneous with the act, not before, in opposition to the Mu'tazila who held it precedes. The text also mentions that this facilitated power is a favor from Allah, and that it is additional to the possible power.

Misconception: That human power is a single ability that exists before the act and is independent of divine assistance.
Correction: Maturidi teaches that there are two types: one consists of external means preceding the act, and the other is a direct divine gift that occurs with the act itself.

Kasb: The Maturidi-Ash'ari Dispute
Compares Maturidi and Ash'ari views on kasb (acquisition).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the Maturidi and Ash'ari concepts of kasb (acquisition) and how it relates to human agency.

Core Idea
Maturidi's kasb is choice before the act; Ash'ari's is simultaneous attachment.

The concept of kasb (acquisition) is central to the debate on human action in kalam. This passage presents the Ash'ari view that kasb is the simultaneous attachment of power to the act, and contrasts it with the Maturidi view that kasb is choice (ikhtiyar) which can precede the act. Understanding this distinction clarifies how Maturidi preserves human responsibility while affirming divine creation of acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on intention and causes
Read from 'مقدمة مع سلامة الآلات' to 'بقدرة الله القديمة'. Understand the conditions for obligation: intention, safety of instruments, clarity of causes.
This sets the stage for why kasb is needed.
2
Identify the two definitions of kasb
Note the Ash'ari definition: kasb is the attachment of originated power to the object of power, concomitant with the act. Then note the Maturidi definition: kasb is choice (ikhtiyar) or discrimination (fasl), which can be prior to the act and power.
The key difference is temporal priority.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: why does it matter if kasb is before or with the act? How does each view affect the servant's responsibility? The commentary will elaborate.
This connects to the broader issue of human free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة مع سلامة الآلات المفصل بعد القصد وسلامة الآلات وضحة الأسباب. فالقصد والأسباب هو مناط التكليف، أما الفعل فواقع هو والقدرة الحادثة المقارنة له بالزمان بقدرة الله القديمة، ولولا القصد مع سلامة الأسباب والآلات لما وقعت مقارنة القدرة الحادثة للمقدور، أي لما وجدت القدرة الحادثة ولا الفعل المقارن لها بمقارنة القدرة الحادثة للمقدور في الكسب عند الأشعري، وليس للعبد في عمله سوى الكسب. لكن الكسب عند الماتريدي هو الاختيار أو إن شئت قل هو الفصل. فكسب الأشعري مع الفعل لا قبله لأنه نفس تعلق القدرة الحادثة بالمقدور، أما كسب الماتريدي فيجوز أن يكون سابقاً للفعل والقدرة الحادثة معاً. وإذ ثبت خلق الأفعال ثبت القضاء بها والتقدير لنا على ما عليه من حسن وقبح. فليس القضاء في حقيقته إلا خلق الأشياء والأفعال ووضع كل شيء في موضعه. قال الله تعالى: (فَقَضَاهُنَّ سَبْعَ سَمَاوَاتٍ) بمعنى خلقهن، وعلى ذلك وصف الأفعال. وما القدر إلا الحد الذي عليه يخرج الشيء، وهو جمل كل شيء على ما هو عليه من خير أو شر، أو حسن أو قبح، أو حكمة أو سنة، وذلك معنى قوله تعالى: (إِنَّا كُلَّ شَيْءٍ خَلَقْنَاهُ بِقَدَرٍ). ثم سؤال: فهل معنى ذلك أن الله قضى بالمعاصي والطاعات والخيرات وقدرها؟ ويفرق الماتريدي بين تقدير المعاصي والشرور والقضاء بها وبين فعل المعصية والشر. أما تقديرها والقضاء بها أو خلقها فكله من الله لأن الله خالق كل شيء، أما فعل المعاصي والشرور فليس من الله بل من العبد بقدرته واختياره وقصده.
Introduction: with safety of instruments, the detailed after intention and safety of instruments and clarity of causes. For intention and causes are the basis of obligation; as for the act, it occurs, it and the originated power concomitant with it in time, by the eternal power of Allah. And were it not for the intention along with safety of causes and instruments, the concomitance of the originated power to the object of power would not occur, i.e., the originated power and the act concomitant to it would not exist, by the concomitance of the originated power to the object of power in acquisition (kasb) according to al-Ash'ari, and the servant has in his action only acquisition. But acquisition according to al-Maturidi is choice, or if you wish, it is discrimination. So al-Ash'ari's acquisition is with the act, not before it, because it is the very attachment of the originated power to the object of power. As for Maturidi's acquisition, it is possible for it to be prior to the act and the originated power together. And when the creation of acts is established, the decree (qada') regarding them and the predetermination (taqdir) for us according to what they are of good and evil is established. For decree in its reality is nothing but the creation of things and acts and putting everything in its place. Allah the Exalted said: 'Then He decreed them as seven heavens' meaning He created them, and upon that, acts are described. And al-qadar (predestination) is nothing but the limit upon which the thing emerges, and it is the totality of everything as it is of good or evil, or beauty or ugliness, or wisdom or folly, and that is the meaning of His saying: 'Indeed, We have created everything in due measure.' Then a question: Does that mean that Allah decreed and predestined sins, acts of obedience, and good things? And al-Maturidi distinguishes between the predestination and decree of sins and evils and the act of sin and evil. As for their predestination and decree or their creation, all of it is from Allah because Allah is the creator of everything; as for the act of sins and evils, it is not from Allah but from the servant by his power, choice, and intention.
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This passage clarifies the Maturidi position on acquisition (kasb), divine decree (qada'), and predestination (qadar). It first contrasts Maturidi's view of kasb as choice (which can precede the act) with Ash'ari's view of kasb as simultaneous attachment. Then it defines qada' as creation and qadar as the measure of things. It addresses the question of whether Allah decrees sins: yes, He decrees and creates the act itself, but the performance of sin is from the servant's own power and choice.

Correction: They differ: Ash'ari says kasb is simultaneous with the act; Maturidi says it can be prior.

Checkpoint

B) Choice or discrimination.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi views kasb as a prior choice, while Ash'ari sees it as a simultaneous attachment—this distinction shapes their understanding of human agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action, do you sense your choice (Maturidi's prior kasb) as truly yours, or do you feel it is simply happening through you (Ash'ari's simultaneous kasb)? How does that feeling affect your sense of responsibility before Allah?
?
The passage says intention and sound means are the basis of obligation. Think of a sin you have repeated—was your intention corrupt, or were the means lacking? What would it take for you to set them right starting today?
?
If Maturidi is correct that your choice precedes the act, then you cannot blame your actions on circumstances or Allah's decree alone. What is one area of your life where you have hidden behind qadar to avoid accountability? Will you now claim your choice and change?

Divine Decree and the Problem of Sin
Defines qada' as creation, qadar as measure, and answers whether Allah decrees sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi definitions of qada' and qadar and how they reconcile Allah's creation of everything with human responsibility for sin.

Core Idea
Allah decrees and creates all acts, but the servant performs sin by his own choice.

After establishing the concept of kasb, the passage moves to the broader framework of divine decree (qada') and predestination (qadar). Maturidi defines qada' as creation and qadar as the measure of things. A critical question arises: does this mean Allah decrees sins? The answer distinguishes between Allah's creation of the act (which includes sins) and the servant's actual commission of sin, which is from his own power and choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of qada' and qadar
Read from 'وإذا ثبت خلق الأفعال' to 'بِقَدَرٍ'. Note that qada' is defined as creation and qadar as the limit/measure.
The Quranic verses are used as evidence.
2
Identify the question about sin
The passage asks whether Allah decreed and predestined sins and acts of obedience. Read the question and the answer.
Notice the careful distinction made.
3
Understand the distinction
Maturidi says the predestination and decree (i.e., creation) of sins is from Allah, but the act of sin is from the servant by his power, choice, and intention.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة مع سلامة الآلات المفصل بعد القصد وسلامة الآلات وضحة الأسباب. فالقصد والأسباب هو مناط التكليف، أما الفعل فواقع هو والقدرة الحادثة المقارنة له بالزمان بقدرة الله القديمة، ولولا القصد مع سلامة الأسباب والآلات لما وقعت مقارنة القدرة الحادثة للمقدور، أي لما وجدت القدرة الحادثة ولا الفعل المقارن لها بمقارنة القدرة الحادثة للمقدور في الكسب عند الأشعري، وليس للعبد في عمله سوى الكسب. لكن الكسب عند الماتريدي هو الاختيار أو إن شئت قل هو الفصل. فكسب الأشعري مع الفعل لا قبله لأنه نفس تعلق القدرة الحادثة بالمقدور، أما كسب الماتريدي فيجوز أن يكون سابقاً للفعل والقدرة الحادثة معاً. وإذ ثبت خلق الأفعال ثبت القضاء بها والتقدير لنا على ما عليه من حسن وقبح. فليس القضاء في حقيقته إلا خلق الأشياء والأفعال ووضع كل شيء في موضعه. قال الله تعالى: (فَقَضَاهُنَّ سَبْعَ سَمَاوَاتٍ) بمعنى خلقهن، وعلى ذلك وصف الأفعال. وما القدر إلا الحد الذي عليه يخرج الشيء، وهو جمل كل شيء على ما هو عليه من خير أو شر، أو حسن أو قبح، أو حكمة أو سنة، وذلك معنى قوله تعالى: (إِنَّا كُلَّ شَيْءٍ خَلَقْنَاهُ بِقَدَرٍ). ثم سؤال: فهل معنى ذلك أن الله قضى بالمعاصي والطاعات والخيرات وقدرها؟ ويفرق الماتريدي بين تقدير المعاصي والشرور والقضاء بها وبين فعل المعصية والشر. أما تقديرها والقضاء بها أو خلقها فكله من الله لأن الله خالق كل شيء، أما فعل المعاصي والشرور فليس من الله بل من العبد بقدرته واختياره وقصده.
Introduction: with safety of instruments, the detailed after intention and safety of instruments and clarity of causes. For intention and causes are the basis of obligation; as for the act, it occurs, it and the originated power concomitant with it in time, by the eternal power of Allah. And were it not for the intention along with safety of causes and instruments, the concomitance of the originated power to the object of power would not occur, i.e., the originated power and the act concomitant to it would not exist, by the concomitance of the originated power to the object of power in acquisition (kasb) according to al-Ash'ari, and the servant has in his action only acquisition. But acquisition according to al-Maturidi is choice, or if you wish, it is discrimination. So al-Ash'ari's acquisition is with the act, not before it, because it is the very attachment of the originated power to the object of power. As for Maturidi's acquisition, it is possible for it to be prior to the act and the originated power together. And when the creation of acts is established, the decree (qada') regarding them and the predetermination (taqdir) for us according to what they are of good and evil is established. For decree in its reality is nothing but the creation of things and acts and putting everything in its place. Allah the Exalted said: 'Then He decreed them as seven heavens' meaning He created them, and upon that, acts are described. And al-qadar (predestination) is nothing but the limit upon which the thing emerges, and it is the totality of everything as it is of good or evil, or beauty or ugliness, or wisdom or folly, and that is the meaning of His saying: 'Indeed, We have created everything in due measure.' Then a question: Does that mean that Allah decreed and predestined sins, acts of obedience, and good things? And al-Maturidi distinguishes between the predestination and decree of sins and evils and the act of sin and evil. As for their predestination and decree or their creation, all of it is from Allah because Allah is the creator of everything; as for the act of sins and evils, it is not from Allah but from the servant by his power, choice, and intention.
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This passage clarifies the Maturidi position on acquisition (kasb), divine decree (qada'), and predestination (qadar). It first contrasts Maturidi's view of kasb as choice (which can precede the act) with Ash'ari's view of kasb as simultaneous attachment. Then it defines qada' as creation and qadar as the measure of things. It addresses the question of whether Allah decrees sins: yes, He decrees and creates the act itself, but the performance of sin is from the servant's own power and choice.

Misconception: Some think that if Allah decrees sin, then He is responsible for sin.
Correction: Maturidi distinguishes: Allah creates the act's existence, but the sinful quality comes from the servant's choice.

B) The servant, by his power, choice, and intention.

Key Takeaway
Allah decrees and creates all acts, but the servant is the agent of sin through his own choice and power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

When you stand before Allah on the Day of Judgment, will you still try to blame qadar for your sins? What will you change about your daily decisions so that your choices align with His pleasure, not your desires?

Maturidi's Definition of Qada' and Qadar
Maturidi defines qada' and qadar in relation to creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's definitions of qada' (decree) as the creation of things and qadar (measure) as the precise limit that determines the nature of each created thing.

Core Idea
Qada' is creation; qadar is the precise limit that defines each thing's nature.

This passage from Imam Maturidi clarifies the foundational concepts of divine decree and measure. After establishing that Allah creates all actions, Maturidi explains that qada' is essentially the act of creation itself, while qadar is the specific limit or measure that gives each thing its particular qualities. Together, they affirm Allah's absolute control over all creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Focus on Maturidi's phrasing: he states definitions directly and supports them with Quranic verses.
Notice the Quranic verses: one about the creation of the heavens, another about all things created with measure.
2
Understand the definitions
Identify Maturidi's definitions: qada' is the creation of things and placing them in their proper place; qadar is the limit according to which a thing emerges.
3
Analyze the Quranic evidence
See how the verse about the heavens (interpreted as 'created') supports the definition of qada', and how the verse 'We have created all things with measure' supports qadar.
Maturidi uses the verse فَقَضَاهُنَّ سَبْعَ سَمَاوَاتٍ to mean 'created them as seven heavens'.
4
Reflect on the implications
What does this passage tell us about Allah's relationship to creation? It shows that divine decree is not arbitrary but involves perfect planning and measurement.
This reframes qada' and qadar as active creative processes, not mere predestination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذ ثبت خلق الأفعال ثبت القضاء يكونها والتبدو لنا على ما عليه من حسن وقبح . فليس القضاء في حقيقته إلا خاق الأشياء والأفعال ووضع كل شيء في موضعه . قال الله تعالى : فَقَضَاهُنَّ سَبْعَ سَمَاواتِ " بمعنى خلفهن ، وعلى ذلك وصف الأفعال. وما القدر إلا الحد الذي عليه يخرج الشيء ، وهو جمل كل شيء على ما هو عليه من خير أو شر ، أو حسن أو قبح ، أو حكمة أو سنه ، وذلك معنى قوله تعالى : إِنَّا كُلَّ شَيْءٍ خَلَقْنَاهُ بِقَدَرٍ
And when the creation of actions is established, then decree (al-qada') regarding their existence and their appearance to us as they are of good and evil is established. So the true nature of decree (al-qada') is nothing but the creation of things and actions and placing everything in its proper place. Allah the Exalted said: 'So He completed them as seven heavens' meaning He created them. And likewise are actions described. And measure (al-qadr) is nothing but the limit according to which the thing comes out, and it is the totality of everything as it is of good or evil, or beauty or ugliness, or wisdom or foolishness. And that is the meaning of His saying: 'Indeed, We have created all things with measure (qadar).'
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This passage defines qada' and qadar after establishing that Allah creates all actions. Qada' is essentially the creation of things and actions and placing them in their proper places, supported by the Quranic verse about the creation of the heavens. Qadar is the limit according to which a thing emerges, encompassing everything in its specific quality of good or evil. Thus, qada' and qadar are not arbitrary but reflect the precise determination of all things.

Correction: This passage equates qada' with creation itself, showing that divine decree is the very act of bringing things into existence and placing them in their proper places.

Maturidi defines qada' as Allah's act of creating things and placing them in their proper place, and qadar as the precise limit that determines each thing's nature.

What would change in your heart if you fully surrendered to that reality?

Allah's Decree and Human Sin
Maturidi distinguishes Allah's creation of sins from the servant's act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Maturidi distinguishes Allah's decree and creation of sins from the servant's own act of sin.

Core Idea
Allah creates sins; servants commit them.

A common question arises: if Allah creates everything, is He responsible for sin? Al-Maturidi provides a crucial distinction. This passage clarifies that while creation and decree belong to Allah, the act of sin originates from the servant's own power and choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the two key phrases: one about Allah's role and one about the servant's role.
2
Identify the two aspects
Notice how the passage first asks a question, then gives a distinction. Find the part that describes what is from Allah and what is from the servant.
The distinction hinges on 'taqdir' and 'qada' vs. 'fi'l'.
3
Reflect on moral responsibility
Consider the implication: if Allah creates the sin but the servant performs it, who is morally responsible? Use the passage to answer.
The servant's power, choice, and intention are key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهل معنى ذلك أن الله قضى بالمعاصي بالطاعات والخبرات وقدرها ؟ يفرق الماتريدي بين تقدير المعاصي والشرور والفضاء بها وبين فعل المعصية والشر. أما تقديرها والفضاء بها أو خلقها فكاه من الله لأن الله خالق كل شيء أما فعل المعاصي والشرور فليس من الله بل من العبد بقدرته واختياره وقصدد
Does that mean that Allah decreed sins, acts of obedience, and good deeds and measured them? Al-Maturidi distinguishes between the measurement (taqdir) of sins and evils and decreeing (qada') with them, and between the act of sin and evil. As for their measurement and decreeing with them or their creation, all of that is from Allah because Allah is the Creator of everything. As for the act of sins and evils, it is not from Allah but from the servant by his power, choice (ikhtiyar), and intention (qasd).
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This passage answers a potential theological question: does Allah's decree of sins mean He wills them? Maturidi distinguishes between two aspects: the measurement and creation of sins (which belong to Allah as Creator of all things) and the actual committing of sins (which is from the human servant by his own power, choice, and intention). Thus, while Allah creates the act as a possibility and determines its limits, the moral responsibility for the sinful act lies with the human agent.

Maturidi's Critique of Mu'tazilite Human Power as Dualism
Maturidi critiques Mu'tazilite human power as dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazilite doctrine of human power logically leads to dualism and contradicts divine unity.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilite human power implies a second creator, violating tawhid.

Maturidi takes on the Mu'tazilite claim that humans have an independent power to act against God's knowledge. He exposes the logical consequence: if a servant can originate an act contrary to God's will, that power becomes a second, rival creative force. This undermines the very foundation of tawhid and aligns with dualist (zanadiqa) thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to how Maturidi builds his argument from the Mu'tazilite premise to its shocking conclusion.
The Arabic may contain OCR errors, so cross-reference with the translation.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite premise
Find where Maturidi states the Mu'tazilite view: that the servant has power over an external act, even a disbeliever can choose to believe. Underline this claim.
This premise is the starting point of the critique.
3
Trace the logical implication
Notice how Maturidi argues that such independent power would amount to a second god. He says: 'if there were another god' — meaning this power functions like a deity. Why does he equate this with dualism?
Think about the nature of tawhid: only Allah has absolute creative power.
4
Examine the contradictions
In the second part, Maturidi points out inconsistencies: the Mu'tazila deny human power in some areas but affirm it in others, and they claim God can't prevent evil acts despite willing otherwise. How does this further show their error?
This shows the Mu'tazilite position is internally inconsistent.
5
Connect to tawhid
Reflect on how the proof for tawhid (that only God creates) refutes both the Mu'tazilite and the dualist positions. What is the correct understanding of human power according to Maturidi?
The correct view is that all power and actions ultimately belong to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من مما علم الله أن يكون؛ مذهب المعتزلة أن العبد يقدر على فعل خارج إذ كل من هو في علم الله أن يكون كافرا يقدر على أن يكون مؤمنا، وحقيقة فأوجب ذلك للعبد قدرة إسرار الفعل عن گونه خروج من ينف والانيته، فكذلك لو كان ثمة إله آخر: فهذا لتعلم أن تذهبهم عند التحصيل مذهب الزنادقة؛ إذ الذي به ثبت التوحيد هو الذي ينقض المذهبين جميعا، والله الموفق. ثم لم يثبتون للعبد قدرة في نفي جميع تذبيره من التوالد ودفع وعيده من قوله: {لأملأن جهنم} [الأعراف:18]، ويثبتون فعل جميع الكفرة والأبالسة بغير الذي يريد الله كونه، بل هو يريد أن لا يكون، ويبذل في منع ذلك كل ما في خزائنه حتى لو أراد أن يزيد فيه شيئا لم يقدر عليه، ثم لم يمنع القول بالإله، وقام الخلق على حسب ما فعلوا.
Then from what God knows to be: the doctrine of the Mu'tazila is that the servant has power over an external act, since everyone whom God knows to be a disbeliever has the power to be a believer. In reality, that necessitated for the servant a power of concealing the act from [؟] his kind of exit from negation and his self. So likewise, if there were another god: this is for you to know that their doctrine, when investigated, is the doctrine of the heretics (zanadiqa); for that by which tawhid is established is that which invalidates both doctrines. And God is the giver of success. Then they do not affirm for the servant the power to negate all his management from reproduction and to repel His threat from His saying: 'I will surely fill Hell' (Quran 7:18). And they affirm the acts of all disbelievers and devils by other than what God wills to be. Rather, He wills that it not be, and He exerts in preventing that all that is in His treasuries, until if He wanted to increase something in it, He would not be able. Then they do not prevent the saying of a god, and creation came into being according to what they did.
Source pages p. 689

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the Mu'tazilite position on human power (qadar). Maturidi begins by stating the Mu'tazilite view: the servant (human) has the power to perform an external act that is independent of God's will, because even someone whom God knows will be a disbeliever has the power to become a believer. This, according to Maturidi, necessitates for the servant a kind of power that can override God's knowledge or will, which is akin to claiming an independent creator. If this were true, then effectively there would be another god alongside God—the human's autonomous power. Thus the Mu'tazilite position, when reduced, is actually the doctrine of the heretics (zanadiqa, i.e., dualists). The very proof used to establish tawhid (divine unity) is what invalidates both the dualist and Mu'tazilite views. Maturidi then elaborates: the Mu'tazila deny the servant the power to negate God's overall management (tadbīr) in areas like reproduction (tawālud) and to repel God's threat of filling Hell, meaning they limit human power. Yet they affirm that the acts of all disbelievers and devils occur contrary to what God wills—God wills them not to exist, but they do. They even claim that God exhausts His treasures to prevent these acts but cannot increase His power. Despite all this, they still do not deny the existence of a god, but their position implies that creation occurs according to human acts, not solely by God's will. This undermines tawhid.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
قدرة العبد
فعل خارج
التوحيد
الزنادقة
التدبير
الوعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mu'tazilite view merely emphasizes human responsibility and doesn't affect divine unity.
Correction: Maturidi shows that their position logically requires an independent power in humans that rivals God's creative capacity, thus undermining tawhid and aligning with dualism.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi equate the Mu'tazilite view on human power with dualism?
A) Because Mu'tazila believe in two eternal gods.
B) Because they attribute to humans an independent power to act contrary to God's will.
C) Because they deny human freedom altogether.
D) Because they affirm that God is the sole creator of all acts.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite doctrine of human power must be rejected because it conflicts with Allah's absolute unity and power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever acted as if your own power to decide is completely independent of Allah's will, forgetting that every ability you have is created and sustained by Him? How can you cultivate a constant awareness that your power is subordinate to His?
?
When you make a choice that contradicts what you know Allah has commanded, do you subtly believe that you have 'overpowered' Allah's decree? How does the Mu'tazilite position—attributing independent power to humans—resemble this attitude, and what does that reveal about your heart?
?
If you truly believed that no act occurs except by Allah's will and power, how would that change your response to your own sins and the sins of others? Would you despair less or take responsibility more? Ponder one specific area in your life where you need to surrender the illusion of independent control.

Human Action and Divine Grace
Maturidi explains the foundation of righteousness and corruption in human action while attributing the means to divine grace.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's balanced view of human responsibility and divine grace in the attainment of righteousness and the occurrence of corruption.

Core Idea
Righteousness and corruption stem from human action, but the means for righteousness are divine favours.

This passage from Imam Maturidi addresses the delicate relationship between human choice and divine sovereignty. Maturidi affirms that the servant's righteousness and corruption originate from his own action, yet he immediately emphasizes that the ability to attain righteousness comes through God's favours. This lesson unpacks how Maturidi maintains both human accountability and divine generosity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Notice the parallel structure: righteousness and corruption both attributed to the servant's action.
2
Identify the two cases
The passage presents two scenarios: the righteous person who attains righteousness through God's means, and the corrupt person who deviates and is left to his choice. Contrast the divine role in each.
3
Reflect on the final phrase
Consider the statement 'the action of enmity' and the closing 'la quwwata illa billah'. How do these reinforce the balance between human responsibility and divine power?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأصل صلاح العبد في الدين إنما هو يفعله ، وكذلك فساده ، والله تعالى بالأسباب التي بها ينال فعل الصلاح عليه أعظم المنن وأجزل النعم ، ومن فسد فهد الأغراض عن الله ، وايتاره شهرته على طاعته، خلى الله بينه وبين ما اختاره لنفسه إذ اثر هواه على أمره وشهوته على طاعته ، والفعل الذي بين له أنه فعل العداوة على ما هو ولا قوة إلا بالله
And the root of the servant's righteousness in religion is only by his action, and likewise his corruption. And Allah the Exalted, by the means through which the action of righteousness is attained, has the greatest favors and the most ample blessings upon him. And whoever becomes corrupt, he deviates from the purposes of Allah, and his preference of his fame over His obedience – Allah leaves him between him and what he chose for himself, since he preferred his desire over His command and his lust over His obedience. And the action which he made clear to him that it is the action of enmity upon what it is. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 723

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of human action in relation to divine grace. Maturidi states that the foundation (aṣl) of a servant's righteousness in religion is by his own action (yaf'aluhu), and likewise corruption. However, God provides the means (asbāb) for attaining righteousness, and these are the greatest favors and blessings. When a person becomes corrupt, they deviate from God's purposes, preferring their own fame over obedience. God then leaves them to what they have chosen for themselves, because they have preferred their desires and lusts over His command and obedience. The final phrase 'the action which He made clear to him that it is the action of enmity' indicates that God has clarified the nature of sinful actions. The balance between human choice and divine sovereignty is maintained: the action is attributed to the servant, but the means and grace are from God.

Key Terms
الصلاح
الفساد
المنن
الاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Maturidi is advocating pure free will, where human actions are entirely independent of God.
Correction: Maturidi affirms human action but grounds the means for righteousness in divine grace, and for corruption, God leaves the person to his own choice, implying divine permission and ultimate sovereignty.

Checkpoint

D) God takes away his ability to choose

Key Takeaway
Righteousness and corruption originate from human action, but the means to righteousness are divine favors from Allah.

The passage says Allah leaves the one who prefers fame over obedience. What is the 'fame' (شهرته) you most crave? Are you willing to surrender that for the sake of Allah’s pleasure? If not, what steps will you take tonight to confront that attachment?

Determinism Undermines the Astrologers' Own Claim
Maturidi refutes astrologers' determinism by revealing its self-contradiction and absurd consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the astrologers' own deterministic claim to refute their position and argue for a wise Creator.

Core Idea
If all actions are compelled, then astrologers' own statements are equally compelled and thus unreliable.

Maturidi now turns the astrologers' own argument against them. They claimed that human actions are compelled by nature or stars, leaving no free choice. But if that were true, Maturidi argues, then their own statements about the stars would also be compelled – not based on knowledge. This leads to a self-defeating position that ultimately points to the existence of a single, wise Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and the translation to get the full flow of Maturidi's argument without interrupting it. Let the text's own logic sink in first.
2
Identify the self-refutation
Notice how Maturidi points out that by claiming they have no choice (lā ikhtiyār), the astrologers admit they are compelled (mudṭarrūn) in what they say. Their denial of opponents is also compelled, leading to a contradiction.
This is the core of his refutation.
3
Follow the two consequences
Maturidi presents two aspects (wajhān): either the astrologers' statement falls and monotheism stands, or they deny direct observation (ʿiyān) and choice (ikhtiyār) that every rational person knows. The latter is absurd.
The two options show the dilemma.
4
Examine the empirical examples
Maturidi gives examples: if actions were compelled by nature or stars, people would not avoid harm or seek pleasure, but they do. Also, a single nature cannot produce opposite actions like cooling and heating, good and evil. This points to a wise Creator.
These examples challenge the determinist claim.
5
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that the origin of actions is not from a thing with a nature (ṭabʿ) but from a knowing, wise Creator. The analogy of the pushed man illustrates that compulsion removes responsibility, which we do not observe.
Thus determinism is false and monotheism is established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن هؤلاء قد أقروا بقولهم أن ليس لهم قول؛ لأنهم زعموا أن لا اختيار لهم: لكنهم مضطرون فيما يقولون، وكذلك خصومهم فيما يكذبونهم، فيكون ذلك التكاذب والتناقض من هذا المدير، ومن ذلك، ومن ذلك قدر قوله فهو لم يقل عند نفسه. وفي ذلك وجهان: أحدهما سقوط قوله فيبقى قول الموحدين، والثاني إنكاره العيان والاختيار الذي يعلمه كل أحد وكل عاقل، ومن أنكر العيان الذي يحيط به حسه، ثم يدعي لا يبلغه حسه بالذي أنكر مما أدركه حسه فهو بحمد الله مكفي المؤونة حقيق بالهجر: وبالله المعونة. ولو كانت الأحوال مدفوعة إليها لما ترك أحد الأكل والشرب خوفا، ولما أقدم عليها لشهوة، ولما أصاب لشيء من ذلك لذة، وكل ذلك موجود فيما عليه، حتى كان فيمن عظم من ذلك أقل منه فيمن صغر، ولو كان بالطبيعة أو اتصال بالنجوم يجب أن يكون على كل قلب به. وبعد، فإن خروج الأفعال المختلفة وأحوالها محال وجودها من ذي طبع كالتبريد والتسخين والشر والخير، فثبت أن ليس أصل شيء منه بذي طبع ولكن بعليم حكيم جعل كل شيء على ذلك بالخلقة والوجود، ولو كانت الأفعال بالدفع لم يمكن الفاعل الامتناع كالمدفوع في قفاه، والذي يهوي من فوق بيت.
Moreover, these people have acknowledged by their statement that they have no statement; because they claimed that they have no choice: but they are compelled (mudṭarrūn) in what they say, and likewise their opponents in what they deny them. So that mutual denial and contradiction would be from this director, and from that, and from that is the measure of his statement: he did not speak from himself. In that are two aspects: one is the fall of his statement, so the statement of the monotheists remains; the second is his denial of direct observation (al-‘iyān) and choice (al-ikhtiyār) which every person and every rational being knows. And whoever denies the direct observation that his sense encompasses, then claims that his sense does not reach what he denied of what his sense perceived—he is, by the praise of God, spared the trouble, worthy of abandonment; and with God is the help. And if states were compelled upon them [i.e., the stars?], no one would leave eating and drinking out of fear, nor would he approach them out of desire, nor would he attain pleasure from any of that; and all of that exists in what he is upon, so much so that there is in one who is great less of that than in one who is small. And if it were by nature (al-ṭab‘) or connection to the stars, it would have to be upon every heart with it. And after that, the emergence of different actions and their states is impossible from a thing with a single nature (ṭab‘), like cooling and heating, evil and good. So it is established that the origin of none of it is from a thing with a nature, but from a knowing, wise one who made everything upon that by creation and existence. And if actions were by compulsion (al-daf‘), the agent could not refrain, like one pushed from behind, or one who falls from above a house.
Source pages p. 745

Commentary
This final passage contains multiple arguments. First, Maturidi uses a self-refutation argument: the astrologers claim they have no free will (lā ikhtiyār), so they are compelled (mudṭarrūn) in what they say. Their denial of their opponents is also compelled, leading to a contradiction. This has two consequences: either their statement falls and monotheism remains, or they deny observation and choice that every rational person knows. The latter is absurd, so the monotheistic view stands. Next, he gives empirical examples: if actions were compelled by nature or stars, people would not avoid harm or seek pleasure—but they do. Finally, he argues that a single nature cannot produce opposite actions (hot/cold, good/evil); thus the source must be a wise Creator who creates all things. The analogy of the pushed man shows that compulsion removes responsibility, but we observe free choice. Thus the argument refutes both determinism and naturalism, affirming a single wise Creator.

Key Terms
اختيار (ikhtiyār)
اضطرار (idtirār)
عيان (ʿiyān)
طبع (ṭabʿ)
دفع (dafʿ)
موحد (muwaḥḥid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is arguing for absolute free will.
Correction: Maturidi is not arguing for absolute free will; he rejects the claim that all actions are compelled by nature or stars, but he does not deny divine power. He affirms that actions are from a wise Creator who creates all things.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude from the fact that different actions like cooling and heating cannot come from a single nature?
A) Nature can produce opposites
B) The origin must be from a knowing, wise Creator
C) Actions are illusory
D) There is no Creator

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that if determinism were true, all statements would be equally compelled, including the astrologers', which contradicts their claim to knowledge; thus the existence of a wise Creator is necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The astrologers claimed they had no choice, so their words were meaningless. Are there areas in your life where you speak or act as if you have no choice—blaming fate, circumstances, or others—rather than taking responsibility? What one decision today can you make with full awareness that you are choosing, not compelled?
?
Maturidi asks us to consider: if we were truly compelled, we would never act out of fear or desire. But you do. When you last chose to avoid something harmful or pursue something beneficial, did you acknowledge that you had a choice, or did you pretend you didn't? What would change if you fully owned that choice?
?
The passage ends by saying the agent under compulsion is like one pushed from behind or falling from a height—powerless to stop. Where in your life have you felt like you were falling without control? What would it mean to believe that God has given you the capacity to choose, even in that situation?

The Self-Defeating Claim of Determinists
Maturidi refutes determinists by exposing the self-contradiction in their claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi exposes the logical self-contradiction in the determinist position, showing that their denial of free will undermines their own argument.

Core Idea
A claim that all actions are compelled is itself a compelled claim, making it unreliable.

Maturidi begins by pointing out the immediate self-defeat in the determinist's assertion. If they claim to have no choice, then their own statement is involuntary and cannot be taken as a valid argument. This short passage lays out the inner contradiction, leading to two consequences that leave the monotheist position standing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi's refutation is concise and direct.
Pay attention to the technical terms like 'ikhtiyar' (choice) and 'idtirar' (compulsion).
2
Identify the self-defeating point
Look at how the determinists' claim that they have no choice contradicts the fact that they are making a statement. How does this undermine their argument?
The key is that if their statement is compelled, it cannot be trusted.
3
Consider the two consequences
The passage mentions two aspects: the fall of their statement and the denial of sensory evidence. Reflect on how each of these reinforces the refutation.
The second consequence is a reductio ad absurdum regarding sense perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن هؤلاء قد أقروا بقولهم أن ليس لهم قول؛ لأنهم زعموا أن لا اختيار لهم: لكنهم مضطرون فيما يقولون، وكذلك خصومهم فيما يكذبونهم، فيكون ذلك التكاذب والتناقض من هذا المدير، ومن ذلك، ومن ذلك قدر قوله فهو لم يقل عند نفسه. وفي ذلك وجهان: أحدهما سقوط قوله فيبقى قول الموحدين، والثاني إنكاره العيان والاختيار الذي يعلمه كل أحد وكل عاقل، ومن أنكر العيان الذي يحيط به حسه، ثم يدعى لا يبلغه حسه بالذي أنكر مما أدركه حسه فهو بحمد الله مكفى.
Moreover, these people have affirmed by their statement that they have no statement; because they claimed that they have no choice: but they are compelled in what they say, and likewise their opponents in what they deny them, so that mutual denial and contradiction is from this director, and from that, and from that the measure of his statement, so he did not speak of his own accord. And in that there are two aspects: one: the fall of his statement, so the statement of the monotheists remains; and the second: his denial of the sensory evidence and choice that every individual and every rational person knows, and whoever denies the sensory evidence that his sense encompasses, then claims that his sense does not reach that which he denied from what his sense perceived, then he is, praise be to God, refuted.
Source pages p. 745

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who claim humans have no free will (determinists). The author points out the self-contradiction: they make a statement but claim they have no choice in making it. This implies their own statement is involuntary and thus meaningless. Two consequences follow: either their statement is invalid, leaving the monotheist position (which likely affirms human responsibility) standing, or they are denying the obvious sensory evidence of choice that everyone experiences. Denying clear sense perception is absurd and merits being dismissed as refuted.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument only applies to verbal statements, but it extends to all actions.
Correction: If one claims all actions are compelled, then their own argument is compelled and thus unreliable.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes determinism by showing that the determinist's own claim is self-defeating, as it denies the very choice needed to make a meaningful argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Two Opposing Views on Actions of Creatures
Maturidi presents two opposing views on human actions and their justifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main opposing views regarding the reality of human actions: the metaphorical view and the view that denies real human agency to avoid shirk.

Core Idea
The debate over human actions hinges on whether they are real or merely metaphorical.

Maturidi begins by presenting the disagreement among Muslim groups over the status of human actions. He outlines two positions: one that considers actions metaphorical for creatures, attributing true causality to God alone, and another that denies real human agency to prevent associating partners with God. This sets the stage for his own solution.

Guided Path
1
Read the disagreement
Read the passage carefully once without analysis. Note that it describes a difference among 'claimants to Islam' regarding actions of creatures.
2
Identify the first view
The first view makes actions metaphorical for creatures and real for God. Note the reasons given: attribution to God is obligatory, and many things attributed to creatures in the Quran are actually metaphorical.
3
Identify the second view
The second view argues that real actions for creatures would imply similarity to God's creation, which is negated in the Quran. Also, it would lead to calling creatures 'creator', which is universally denied.
4
Compare the views
Notice that both views attempt to preserve divine sovereignty, but they differ on whether human actions are real or metaphorical. This tension is what Maturidi will resolve.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله: اختلف منتحلو الإسلام في أفعال الخلق، فمنهم من جعلها لهم مجازا، وحقيقتها لله بأوجه: أحدها وجوب إضافتها إلى الله، على ما أضيف إليه خلق كل شيء في الجملة، فلم يجز أن يكون الإضافة إلى الله مجازا؛ لأنه الفاعل الحق والقادر الذي لا يعجزه شيء، وفي ذلك إخراج عن قدرته وإزالة عن حقيقة فعله. وقد أضيف كثير مما لا يشك على أن الله هو منشئه إلى العباد بالحرف الذي هو حرف العبادة عن الأفعال كالموت والحياة، والطول والقصر، والحركة والسكون، والاجتماع والتفرق، والله سبحانه لكل ذلك فاعل، وعلى كله قادر، فمثله ما ذكرنا. وإضافة ذلك في القرآن ظاهر. وذهب هؤلاء في التعذيب ونحو ذلك إلى أن له الخلق والأمر بكليته له في ذلك ما شاء، على ما قدر كل مالك في ملكه ما له فيه، وإن كان ذلك كله على هذا القول مجازي. والثاني أن بتحقيق الفعل لغيره تشابها في الفعل، وقد نفى الله ذلك بقوله: {جَعَلُوا لِلَّهِ شُرَكَاءَ خَلَقُوا كَخَلْقِهِ فَتَشَابَهَ الْخَلْقُ عَلَيْهِم}، وإذا لم يكن حقيقة الأملاك في الجواهر وفي الإلزام يقع تشابه في الملك، فمثله في الأفعال. وأيضا أنه لو جعل للعبد إيجاد وإخراج من العدم لكان في معنى خلق، فيلزم اسم خالق، وذلك مما أباه الجميع؛ حيث قالوا: لا خالق إلا الله. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: وعندنا لازم تحقيق الفعل لهم بالسمع والعقل والضرورة التي يصير دافع ذلك مكابرا. فأما السمع فله وجهان: الأمر به والنهي عنه، والثاني الوعيد فيه والوعد له، على تسمية ذلك في كل هذا فعلا، من نحو قوله: {اعْمَلُوا مَا شِئْتُمْ}، وقوله: {وَافْعَلُوا الْخَيْرَ}، وفي الجزاء {يُرِيهِمُ اللهُ أَعْمَالَهُمْ}.
The jurist, may God have mercy on him, said: The claimants to Islam differed regarding the actions of creatures. Among them are those who made them metaphorical for them, and their reality belongs to God in several aspects: One of them is the obligation of attributing them to God, based on the attribution of the creation of everything in general to Him, so it is not permissible for the attribution to God to be metaphorical; because He is the true agent and the powerful one whom nothing incapacitates, and that would be an exclusion from His power and a removal from the reality of His act. And many things that there is no doubt that God is their originator have been attributed to servants by the letter of attribution of actions (e.g., death and life, length and shortness, motion and stillness, gathering and separation), and God is the agent of all that and powerful over all of it, and the like of what we mentioned. And their attribution in the Quran is apparent. And these [people] went, regarding punishment and the like, to the view that to Him belongs creation and command entirely, He has in that what He wills, just as every owner is empowered in his ownership concerning what he possesses, even if all that according to this view is metaphorical. The second [view] is that making the action real for another [results in] similarity in action, and God has negated that in His saying: {They have set up partners for God who created like His creation, so the creation became similar to them.} And if there is no reality of ownership in substances and in imposition, similarity in ownership occurs, so likewise in actions. And also, if a servant were given bringing into existence and bringing out from non-existence, it would be in the meaning of creation, and the name 'creator' would be necessary, and that is something all abhor; for they said: There is no creator but God. The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: According to us, the realization of action for them is necessary by revelation, reason, and necessity, such that one who rejects it becomes obstinate. As for revelation, it has two aspects: the command to do it and the prohibition from it, and the second [aspect] is the threat concerning it and the promise for it, based on calling all that 'action'. For example, His saying: {Do whatever you will}, and His saying: {Do good}, and in recompense: {God will show them their deeds}.
Source pages p. 824

Commentary
This passage presents the disagreement among Muslim sects regarding the actions of creatures (af‘al al-khalq) and then states the Maturidi position. First, the author (referred to as 'al-faqih'—likely the Maturidi himself) describes two main opposing views. The first view is that actions are attributed to creatures metaphorically (majāzan), while the true agent is God alone. This view gives several reasons: (1) Actions must be attributed to God because He is the true agent and capable of everything; attributing them to creatures metaphorically would detract from His power. However, the author notes that many things (like death, life, etc.) are explicitly attributed to creatures in the Quran, yet God is the real agent. These proponents consider such attributions metaphorical (majāzī). (2) The second view considered is that affirming real action for another would imply similarity (tashābuh) with God's creating, which God has negated in the verse about setting up partners. If a servant could bring something from non-existence, that would be like creation, leading to the name 'creator' which is universally denied. Thus, they deny real efficiency for creatures. Then the Maturidi position is introduced with 'Qāla al-shaykh raḥimahu Allāh' (the master): According to us, the realization of action for creatures is necessary (lāzim) by revelation (sam‘), reason ('aql), and necessity (ḍarūra). One who rejects this becomes obstinate (mukābiran). Revelation supports this in two ways: commands and prohibitions (which imply the action is real), and threats and promises (which are based on the action). The author cites Quranic verses: 'Do what you will' (41:40) and 'Do good' (22:77), and 'God will show them their deeds' (another verse). These show that actions are truly attributed to creatures by divine legislative discourse. The Maturidi stance is that while God is the ultimate creator, creatures have real (ḥaqīqī) actions that they perform, as evidenced by scriptural command and prohibition, rational necessity, and evident necessity of daily experience.

Key Terms
أفعال الخلق (af‘āl al-khalq)
مجاز (majāz)
حقيقة (ḥaqīqa)
خالق (khāliq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first view presented, why are actions attributed to creatures metaphorically?
A) To affirm that creatures are the true agents.
B) Because God is the true agent and attributing actions literally to creatures would diminish His power.
C) Because actions are not real at all.
D) Because the Quran clearly states that actions belong to creatures.

Key Takeaway
Muslim groups disagreed on whether human actions are real or metaphorical, both trying to safeguard divine sovereignty, but each approach has serious theological issues.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you truly believe that every aspect of that action is under God's control? How does that belief change the way you repent or seek forgiveness?
?
The passage warns that claiming real independent action for humans could lead to shirk. In your daily life, where do you feel most tempted to attribute your successes entirely to yourself? What would it look like to instead attribute them to God?
?
If all actions are ultimately from God, does that make you more or less careful about the choices you make? How can you actively remind yourself that your actions are a test, not a creation of your own?

Maturidi's Proof for Real Human Actions
Maturidi states his position and provides three proofs for real human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi proof that human actions are truly attributable to them, based on revelation, reason, and necessity.

Core Idea
Maturidi proves real human agency through divine commands, threats, and rational necessity.

Having presented the conflicting views, Maturidi now asserts his own position: human actions are truly theirs (ḥaqīqatan), supported by revelation, reason, and necessity. He shows that the very structure of Islamic law presupposes real human agency, and rejecting it amounts to obstinacy.

Guided Path
1
Read Maturidi's claim
Read the passage where Maturidi states his position: 'According to us, the realization of action for them is necessary by revelation, reason, and necessity.' Note the strong language: rejecting this makes one obstinate.
2
Examine the proof from revelation
Revelation has two aspects: commands/prohibitions and threats/promises. These only make sense if the servant can actually perform or avoid actions. Reflect on the Quranic verses cited.
Note that commands imply ability and real action.
3
Understand reason and necessity
Maturidi also appeals to reason and evident necessity. Daily experience shows that humans are agents of their actions, and reason confirms this. Denying it contradicts clear intuition.
4
Synthesize the proof
Combine all three proofs: revelation commands and promises, reason shows logical coherence, and necessity is undeniable. Together they establish that human actions are real.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله: اختلف منتحلو الإسلام في أفعال الخلق، فمنهم من جعلها لهم مجازا، وحقيقتها لله بأوجه: أحدها وجوب إضافتها إلى الله، على ما أضيف إليه خلق كل شيء في الجملة، فلم يجز أن يكون الإضافة إلى الله مجازا؛ لأنه الفاعل الحق والقادر الذي لا يعجزه شيء، وفي ذلك إخراج عن قدرته وإزالة عن حقيقة فعله. وقد أضيف كثير مما لا يشك على أن الله هو منشئه إلى العباد بالحرف الذي هو حرف العبادة عن الأفعال كالموت والحياة، والطول والقصر، والحركة والسكون، والاجتماع والتفرق، والله سبحانه لكل ذلك فاعل، وعلى كله قادر، فمثله ما ذكرنا. وإضافة ذلك في القرآن ظاهر. وذهب هؤلاء في التعذيب ونحو ذلك إلى أن له الخلق والأمر بكليته له في ذلك ما شاء، على ما قدر كل مالك في ملكه ما له فيه، وإن كان ذلك كله على هذا القول مجازي. والثاني أن بتحقيق الفعل لغيره تشابها في الفعل، وقد نفى الله ذلك بقوله: {جَعَلُوا لِلَّهِ شُرَكَاءَ خَلَقُوا كَخَلْقِهِ فَتَشَابَهَ الْخَلْقُ عَلَيْهِم}، وإذا لم يكن حقيقة الأملاك في الجواهر وفي الإلزام يقع تشابه في الملك، فمثله في الأفعال. وأيضا أنه لو جعل للعبد إيجاد وإخراج من العدم لكان في معنى خلق، فيلزم اسم خالق، وذلك مما أباه الجميع؛ حيث قالوا: لا خالق إلا الله. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: وعندنا لازم تحقيق الفعل لهم بالسمع والعقل والضرورة التي يصير دافع ذلك مكابرا. فأما السمع فله وجهان: الأمر به والنهي عنه، والثاني الوعيد فيه والوعد له، على تسمية ذلك في كل هذا فعلا، من نحو قوله: {اعْمَلُوا مَا شِئْتُمْ}، وقوله: {وَافْعَلُوا الْخَيْرَ}، وفي الجزاء {يُرِيهِمُ اللهُ أَعْمَالَهُمْ}.
The jurist, may God have mercy on him, said: The claimants to Islam differed regarding the actions of creatures. Among them are those who made them metaphorical for them, and their reality belongs to God in several aspects: One of them is the obligation of attributing them to God, based on the attribution of the creation of everything in general to Him, so it is not permissible for the attribution to God to be metaphorical; because He is the true agent and the powerful one whom nothing incapacitates, and that would be an exclusion from His power and a removal from the reality of His act. And many things that there is no doubt that God is their originator have been attributed to servants by the letter of attribution of actions (e.g., death and life, length and shortness, motion and stillness, gathering and separation), and God is the agent of all that and powerful over all of it, and the like of what we mentioned. And their attribution in the Quran is apparent. And these [people] went, regarding punishment and the like, to the view that to Him belongs creation and command entirely, He has in that what He wills, just as every owner is empowered in his ownership concerning what he possesses, even if all that according to this view is metaphorical. The second [view] is that making the action real for another [results in] similarity in action, and God has negated that in His saying: {They have set up partners for God who created like His creation, so the creation became similar to them.} And if there is no reality of ownership in substances and in imposition, similarity in ownership occurs, so likewise in actions. And also, if a servant were given bringing into existence and bringing out from non-existence, it would be in the meaning of creation, and the name 'creator' would be necessary, and that is something all abhor; for they said: There is no creator but God. The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: According to us, the realization of action for them is necessary by revelation, reason, and necessity, such that one who rejects it becomes obstinate. As for revelation, it has two aspects: the command to do it and the prohibition from it, and the second [aspect] is the threat concerning it and the promise for it, based on calling all that 'action'. For example, His saying: {Do whatever you will}, and His saying: {Do good}, and in recompense: {God will show them their deeds}.
Source pages p. 824

Commentary
This passage presents the disagreement among Muslim sects regarding the actions of creatures (af‘al al-khalq) and then states the Maturidi position. First, the author (referred to as 'al-faqih'—likely the Maturidi himself) describes two main opposing views. The first view is that actions are attributed to creatures metaphorically (majāzan), while the true agent is God alone. This view gives several reasons: (1) Actions must be attributed to God because He is the true agent and capable of everything; attributing them to creatures metaphorically would detract from His power. However, the author notes that many things (like death, life, etc.) are explicitly attributed to creatures in the Quran, yet God is the real agent. These proponents consider such attributions metaphorical (majāzī). (2) The second view considered is that affirming real action for another would imply similarity (tashābuh) with God's creating, which God has negated in the verse about setting up partners. If a servant could bring something from non-existence, that would be like creation, leading to the name 'creator' which is universally denied. Thus, they deny real efficiency for creatures. Then the Maturidi position is introduced with 'Qāla al-shaykh raḥimahu Allāh' (the master): According to us, the realization of action for creatures is necessary (lāzim) by revelation (sam‘), reason ('aql), and necessity (ḍarūra). One who rejects this becomes obstinate (mukābiran). Revelation supports this in two ways: commands and prohibitions (which imply the action is real), and threats and promises (which are based on the action). The author cites Quranic verses: 'Do what you will' (41:40) and 'Do good' (22:77), and 'God will show them their deeds' (another verse). These show that actions are truly attributed to creatures by divine legislative discourse. The Maturidi stance is that while God is the ultimate creator, creatures have real (ḥaqīqī) actions that they perform, as evidenced by scriptural command and prohibition, rational necessity, and evident necessity of daily experience.

Key Terms
السمع (al-sam‘)
العقل (al-‘aql)
الضرورة (al-ḍarūra)
فاعل (fā‘il)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that affirming real human actions contradicts God's sole creative power, leading to shirk.
Correction: Maturidi distinguishes between true creation (khalq) which belongs only to God, and human action (fi‘l) which is real but not independent creation; God is the ultimate creator of all actions, but humans acquire them.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, which of the following is NOT a proof for real human actions?
A) Revelation (commands and prohibitions)
B) Reason
C) Consensus of the scholars (ijma‘)
D) Necessity (ḍarūra)

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that human actions are truly theirs, proven by divine commands and promises, rational analysis, and everyday necessity—without compromising God's sole creative power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of obedience, do you truly feel that it is your action, or do you passively attribute it to God? How does this affect your sense of responsibility and motivation?
?
If you believed that your sins were not truly your own doing, would you still feel remorse and seek forgiveness? Examine your heart: Do you use divine predestination as an excuse to avoid accountability?
?
Maturidi says denying human agency is 'obstinate.' Think of a moment when you acted against your desires to obey God. Did you experience a sense of personal struggle and choice? How can that awareness deepen your gratitude and effort?
